The Book of the Remembrance of Death and What Comes After It
This is the Tenth Book of the Quarter of the Deliverances, and with it concludes the book *The Revival of the Religious Sciences*.
In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful.

Praise be to God, Who broke with death the necks of tyrants, shattered with it the backs of emperors, and cut short with it the aspirations of Caesars whose hearts ever shrank from the remembrance of death, until the True Promise came to them and cast them into the pit. Thus, they were transferred from palaces to graves, from the light of cradles to the darkness of tombs, from sporting with maidens and youths to enduring vermin and worms, from enjoying food and drink to wallowing in dust, from the comfort of companionship to the desolation of solitude, and from soft beds to a grievous place of demise. So consider: did they find in death any fortress or might, or take behind it any screen or protection? And consider: "Do you perceive of them anyone or hear from them a whisper?" So glory be to Him Who is alone in subjugation and mastery, Who reserves for Himself the right to permanence, and Who humbled all categories of creation by what He decreed upon them of annihilation. Then He made death a deliverance for the God-fearing and, in their regard, an appointment for the Meeting; and He made the grave a prison for the wretched and a constricted confinement for them until the Day of Separation and Decree. To Him belongs the bestowal of manifest blessings, and to Him belongs the retribution with overwhelming calamities. To Him is gratitude in the heavens and the earth, and to Him is praise in the First and the Last. And blessings be upon Muhammad, possessor of manifest miracles and brilliant signs, and upon his family and his companions, abundant salutations.

Now then: It is fitting for one whose final destination is death, whose resting place is dust, whose companion is the worm, whose intimates are Munkar and Nakir, whose abode is the grave and whose settlement is the belly of the earth, whose appointment is the Resurrection, and whose outcome is either Paradise or the Fire, that he have no thought except of death, no remembrance except of it, no preparation except for its sake, no planning except concerning it, no aspiration except towards it, no turning aside except to it, no concern except with it, no movement except around it, and no waiting and anticipation except for it. And he is right to consider himself among the dead and to see himself among the inhabitants of the graves. For everything that is to come is near, and the distant is only that which is not coming. The Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him, said: "The intelligent one is he who holds himself to account and works for what is after death." Preparation for a thing will only be facilitated when its remembrance is renewed upon the heart, and its remembrance is only renewed through reminding by listening to what reminds of it and by reflecting upon what alerts to it. We shall mention, concerning the matter of death, its precursors, its consequences, and the states of the Hereafter, the Resurrection, Paradise, and the Fire, what the servant must necessarily remember repeatedly and accompany with reflection and insight, so that it may spur him to preparation. For the departure to what is after death has drawn near, and what remains of life is but little, while mankind is heedless of it: "The reckoning for the people has drawn near, while they are in heedlessness, turning away." We shall mention what pertains to death in two parts:

(The First Part: Concerning its precursors and what follows them up to the Blast of the Trumpet, containing eight chapters:)
Chapter One: On the excellence of remembering death and the encouragement therein.
Chapter Two: On the mention of long hopes and their brevity.
Chapter Three: On the agonies of death and its severity, and the states that are recommended at the time of death.
Chapter Four: On the passing of the Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him, and the Rightly-Guided Caliphs after him.
Chapter Five: On the words of the dying among the Caliphs, rulers, and the righteous.
Chapter Six: On the sayings of the knowers concerning funerals and graves, and the ruling on visiting graves.
Chapter Seven: On the reality of death and what the deceased encounters in the grave up to the Blast of the Trumpet.
Chapter Eight: On what is known of the states of the dead through unveiling in dreams.

(Chapter One: On the remembrance of death and the encouragement to increase in its remembrance)
Know that one who is engrossed in the world, bent upon its delusions, and enamored of its pleasures, his heart is inevitably heedless of the remembrance of death; he does not remember it, and when he is reminded of it, he dislikes it and recoils from it. Those are the ones concerning whom God said: "Say, 'Indeed, the death from which you flee - indeed, it will meet you. Then you will be returned to the Knower of the unseen and the witnessed, and He will inform you about what you used to do.'" Now, people are either engrossed, or a repentant beginner, or a knower who has reached the end. As for the engrossed, he does not remember death, and if he does remember it, he remembers it to lament his worldly life and occupies himself with blaming it. This only increases his distance from God through the remembrance of death. As for the repentant one, he increases in the remembrance of death so that fear and awe may surge from his heart, thereby fulfilling repentance completely. He may dislike death, fearing it might snatch him before the completion of repentance and before preparing his provision, and he is excused in that.

Aversion to death does not fall under His saying, may He be exalted: "Whoever dislikes meeting Allah, Allah dislikes meeting him." For this person does not dislike death and meeting Allah; rather, he fears missing the meeting with Allah due to his own deficiency and shortcoming. He is like one who delays meeting the beloved, being occupied with preparing for the meeting in a manner that pleases him. Thus, he is not considered one who dislikes the meeting. The sign of this is that he is perpetually prepared for it, having no occupation other than it; otherwise, he joins those engrossed in the world. As for the knower of God, he remembers death constantly because it is the appointed time for meeting his Beloved, and a lover never forgets the appointment to meet the beloved. For the most part, such a person finds death slow in coming and loves its arrival, to be freed from the abode of the disobedient and to transfer to the proximity of the Lord of the worlds. As narrated from Hudhayfah, when death approached him, he said: "A beloved has come in a state of need; unsuccessful is he who regrets. O Allah, if You know that poverty is more beloved to me than wealth, sickness more beloved than health, and death more beloved than life, then make death easy for me until I meet You." Therefore, the repentant one is excused in his aversion to death, and this one is excused in his love for death and longing for it. Higher in rank than both is he who entrusts his affair to Allah, the Exalted, so that he chooses for himself neither death nor life, but rather the most beloved thing to him is what is most beloved to his Master. This one has reached, through intense love and devotion, the station of submission and contentment, which is the ultimate goal and end. In any case, there is reward and merit in remembering death. For even the engrossed one benefits from remembering death by turning away from the world, as it spoils his comfort and clouds the purity of his pleasure. Anything that clouds a person's pleasures and desires is among the causes of salvation.

(Explanation of the merit of remembering death in any state)
The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: "Increase your remembrance of the destroyer of pleasures." Its meaning is: spoil your pleasures by its remembrance until your reliance upon them is severed, so that you may turn to Allah, the Exalted. And he, peace be upon him, said: "If the beasts knew of death what the son of Adam knows, you would not eat fat from them." And 'A'ishah, may Allah be pleased with her, said: "O Messenger of Allah, will anyone be resurrected with the martyrs?" He said: "Yes, one who remembers death twenty times a day and night." The cause of all this merit is that remembering death necessitates turning away from the abode of delusion and demands preparation for the Hereafter, while heedlessness of death calls to immersion in worldly desires. And he, peace be upon him, said: "The gift of the believer is death." He said this because the world is a prison for the believer, for he remains in it in hardship from contending with his soul, disciplining his desires, and repelling his devil. Thus, death is a release for him from this torment, and release is a gift in his right. And he, peace be upon him, said: "Death is an expiation for every Muslim." By this, he meant the true Muslim, the sincere believer, from whose tongue and hand the Muslims are safe, and in whom the character of the believers is realized, and who is not stained by sins except minor lapses. Death purifies him from them and expiates them after he has avoided major sins and established the obligatory duties. 'Ata' al-Khurasani said: "The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, passed by a gathering where laughter had become excessive. He said: 'Mix your gathering with the remembrance of that which spoils pleasures.' They said: 'And what is that which spoils pleasures?' He said: 'Death.'" And Anas, may Allah be pleased with him, said: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: "Increase your remembrance of death, for it purifies sins and induces asceticism in the world." And he, peace be upon him, said: "Sufficient is death as a separator." And upon him be peace, said: "Sufficient is death as an admonisher." "The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, went out to the mosque and found people talking and laughing. He said: 'Remember death. By the One in whose hand is my soul, if you knew what I know, you would laugh little and weep much.'" "A man was mentioned in the presence of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, and they praised him well. He said: 'How is your companion's remembrance of death?' They said: 'We hardly ever hear him mention death.' He said: 'Then your companion is not there.'" And Ibn 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with them both, said: I came to the Prophet, peace be upon him, as the tenth of ten. A man from the Ansar said: "Who are the most intelligent and the most honorable of people, O Messenger of Allah?" He said: "Those who remember death most and are most prepared for it. Those are the intelligent ones; they have taken the nobility of this world and the honor of the Hereafter." As for the narrations: Al-Hasan, may Allah have mercy on him, said: "Death has exposed the world, leaving no person of understanding joyful." And al-Rabi' ibn Khuthaym said: "No absent thing awaited by the believer is better for him than death." And he used to say: "Do not make anyone aware of me, and deliver me smoothly to my Lord." And one of the wise wrote to a man from his brothers: "O my brother, beware of death in this abode before you reach an abode where you will wish for death and not find it." And when death was mentioned in the presence of Ibn Sirin, every limb of his would die. And 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz would gather the jurists every night, and they would discuss death.

And the Resurrection and the Hereafter, then they weep as if there were a funeral bier before them. Ibrahim al-Taymi said: "Two things have severed from me the pleasure of this world: the remembrance of death and the standing before God, Mighty and Majestic is He." Ka'b said: "Whoever knows death, the calamities and sorrows of this world become insignificant to him." Mutarrif said: "I saw in a dream, as one sees in sleep, as if a caller was saying in the middle of the Basra mosque: 'The remembrance of death has severed the hearts of the fearful.' By God, you see them only as distraught." Ash'ath said: "We used to visit al-Hasan, and it was only about the Fire, the affair of the Hereafter, and the remembrance of death." Safiyyah, may God be pleased with her, said: "A woman complained to 'A'ishah, may God be pleased with her, about the hardness of her heart. She said: 'Increase your remembrance of death, and your heart will soften.' She did so, and her heart softened, and she came thanking 'A'ishah, may God be pleased with her." And 'Isa, peace be upon him, when death was mentioned in his presence, his skin would drip blood. And Dawud, peace be upon him, when he remembered death and the Resurrection, would weep until his limbs were convulsed, and when he remembered mercy, his soul would return to him. Al-Hasan said: "I have never seen a rational person except that I found him wary of death and sorrowful over it." 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz said to some of the scholars: "Admonish me." He said: "You are not the first caliph to die." He said: "Increase me." He said: "There is none of your forefathers, all the way back to Adam, except that he has tasted death, and your turn has come." So 'Umar wept for that. And al-Rabi' ibn Khuthaym had dug a grave in his house and would sleep in it several times a day, thereby perpetuating the remembrance of death. He used to say: "If the remembrance of death were to depart from my heart for a single moment, it would become corrupt." And Mutarrif ibn 'Abdullah ibn al-Shikhkhir said: "Indeed, this death has embittered the bliss of the people of bliss, so seek a bliss in which there is no death." And 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz said to 'Anbasah: "Increase your remembrance of death, for if your life is ample, it will constrict it for you, and if your life is constricted, it will make it ample for you." And Abu Sulayman al-Darani said: "I said to the mother of Harun: 'Do you love death?' She said: 'No.' I said: 'Why?' She said: 'If I disobeyed a human, I would not desire to meet him, so how could I love to meet Him when I have disobeyed Him?'"

(Clarifying the Path to Realizing the Remembrance of Death)

Know that death is dreadful and its danger is great, and people's heedlessness of it is due to their lack of reflection upon it and remembrance of it. And whoever remembers it does not remember it with an empty heart, but with a heart preoccupied by worldly desires, so the remembrance of death does not benefit his heart. The path in this is for the servant to empty his heart of everything except the remembrance of death, which is before him, like one who intends to travel to a perilous desert or to embark upon the sea, for he thinks of nothing else. When his heart engages with the remembrance of death, it is likely to affect him, and at that point his joy and delight in the world diminish, and his heart is broken. The most effective path in this is to frequently remember his peers and contemporaries who have passed before him, and to recall their death and their resting places beneath the earth, and to recall their images in their positions and states, and to contemplate how the earth has now erased the beauty of their forms, and how their parts have scattered in their graves, and how they have widowed their wives, orphaned their children, squandered their wealth, left their mosques and gatherings empty of them, and their traces have been severed. Whenever a man remembers another man and details in his heart his condition and the manner of his death, and imagines his form, and recalls his vigor, his comings and goings, his contemplation of life and permanence, his forgetfulness of death, his deception by the compliance of means, his reliance on strength and youth, his inclination to laughter and diversion, and his heedlessness of the dreadful death and swift destruction before him—and how he used to come and go, and now his legs and joints have crumbled; and how he used to speak, and now worms have eaten his tongue; and how he used to laugh, and now earth has eaten his teeth; and how he used to arrange for himself what he would not need for ten years, at a time when there was not between him and death but a month, and he was heedless of what was intended for him until death came to him at a time he did not reckon—then the image of the angel was unveiled to him, and the call struck his hearing, either for Paradise or for the Fire. At that point, he looks at himself and sees that he is like them, his heedlessness is like their heedlessness, and his end will be like their end. Abu al-Darda', may God be pleased with him, said: "When you remember the dead, count yourself as one of them." Ibn Mas'ud, may God be pleased with him, said: "The fortunate one is he who is admonished by others." 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz said: "Do you not see that you are preparing every day one who goes forth in the morning or returns in the evening to God, Mighty and Majestic is He, placing him in a crack of the earth, having made the earth his pillow, leaving behind loved ones, and severing means?" Persistence in these thoughts and their like, along with entering graveyards and observing the sick, is what renews the remembrance of death in the heart until it overcomes it, such that it becomes ever-present before his eyes. At that point, it is likely to become firmly established for him, and he withdraws from the abode of delusion. Otherwise, remembrance with the surface of the heart and the tip of the tongue is of little benefit in warning and alerting. Whenever his heart finds delight in anything of...

The world is such that one should constantly remember he must inevitably depart from it. Ibn Mutīʿ once looked at his house, admired its beauty, then wept and said: “By God, were it not for death, I would be delighted with you; and were it not for what we are destined for—the constriction of the graves—our eyes would find contentment in this world.” Then he wept bitterly until his voice rose.

(Chapter Two: On the Length of Hope, the Virtue of Short Hope, Its Cause, and Its Remedy)  
(Virtue of Short Hope)

The Messenger of God (peace be upon him) said to ʿAbd Allāh ibn ʿUmar: “When morning comes, do not anticipate the evening; and when evening comes, do not anticipate the morning. Take from your life for your death, and from your health for your sickness. For you, O ʿAbd Allāh, do not know what your name will be tomorrow.” And ʿAlī (may God honor his face) reported that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: “The two things I fear most for you are following desires and long hope. As for following desires, it turns one away from the truth; and as for long hope, it is love of this world.” Then he said: “Indeed, God gives the world to both those He loves and those He dislikes, but when He loves a servant, He grants him faith. Indeed, religion has its children and the world has its children, so be among the children of religion and not among the children of the world. Indeed, the world has departed, turning its back; indeed, the Hereafter has departed, approaching. Indeed, you are in a day of deeds without reckoning, and indeed you will soon be in a day of reckoning without deeds.”

Umm al-Mundhir said: “The Messenger of God (peace be upon him) once looked upon the people in the evening and said: ‘O people, do you not feel shame before God?’ They said: ‘What is it, O Messenger of God?’ He said: ‘You gather what you do not eat, you hope for what you will not attain, and you build what you will not inhabit.’”

Abū Saʿīd al-Khudrī said: “Usāmah ibn Zayd purchased a slave-girl from Zayd ibn Thābit for one hundred dinars, with payment due in a month. I heard the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) say: ‘Are you not amazed at Usāmah, who buys with payment due in a month? Indeed, Usāmah has long hope. By the One in whose hand is my soul, I have not blinked my eyes without thinking my eyelids would not meet again until God takes my soul; nor have I raised my glance without thinking I would not lower it until I pass away; nor have I taken a bite without thinking I would not swallow it before being choked by death.’ Then he said: ‘O children of Adam, if you understand, count yourselves among the dead. By the One in whose hand is my soul, “What you are promised is sure to come, and you cannot escape it.”’”

From Ibn ʿAbbās (may God be pleased with them both): “The Messenger of God (peace be upon him) would go out to pour water and then wipe himself with dust. I said to him: ‘O Messenger of God, water is close to you.’ He replied: ‘How do I know? Perhaps I will not reach it.’”

It is reported that he (peace be upon him) took three sticks, planted one in front of him, another beside him, and the third farther away. Then he said: “Do you know what this is?” They said: “God and His Messenger know best.” He said: “This is the human, this is his appointed term, and that is his hope. The son of Adam reaches for it, but the appointed term seizes him before the hope.”

He (peace be upon him) said: “The likeness of the son of Adam is that beside him are ninety-nine deaths; if the deaths miss him, he falls into old age.” Ibn Masʿūd said: “This person, and these deaths are pathways around him leading to him, with old age behind the deaths and hope behind old age. So he hopes while these deaths are pathways toward him; whichever one passes by him takes him. If the deaths miss him, old age kills him while he is still awaiting hope.”

ʿAbd Allāh said: “The Messenger of God (peace be upon him) drew for us a square, drew a line through its middle, drew lines beside that line, and drew a line outside it. Then he said: ‘Do you know what this is?’ We said: ‘God and His Messenger know best.’ He said: ‘This is the human—the line in the middle; this is his appointed term surrounding him; these are the calamities—the lines around him—gnawing at him. If this one misses him, that one gnaws at him; and that is hope—the line outside.’”

Anas said: The Messenger of God (peace be upon him) said: “The son of Adam grows old, but two things remain with him: greed and hope.” In another narration: “Two things accompany him: greed for wealth and greed for long life.” And the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) said: “The first of this community were saved through certainty and asceticism, and the last of this community will be ruined by stinginess and hope.”

It is said that while Jesus (peace be upon him) was sitting, an old man was working with a shovel, turning the earth. Jesus said: “O God, remove hope from him.” So the old man put down his shovel and lay down. After a while, Jesus said: “O God, restore hope to him.” The man got up and began to work again. Jesus asked him about that, and he replied: “While I was working, my soul said to me: ‘How long will you work when you are an old man?’ So I threw down the shovel and lay down. Then my soul said to me: ‘By God, you must have sustenance as long as you live.’ So I got up and returned to my shovel.”

Al-Ḥasan said: The Messenger of God (peace be upon him) said: “Do all of you love to enter Paradise?” They said: “Yes, O Messenger of God.” He said: “Shorten your hopes, place your appointed terms before your eyes, and feel true shame before God.” And he (peace be upon him) used to say in his supplication:

O Allah, I seek refuge in You from a worldly life that prevents the good of the Hereafter, and I seek refuge in You from a life that prevents the good of death, and I seek refuge in You from a hope that prevents the good of deeds. The narrations: Mutarrif ibn ‘Abdillah said: If I knew when my appointed time was, I would fear for the loss of my mind. But Allah the Exalted has bestowed favor upon His servants by making them heedless of death. Were it not for this heedlessness, they would not enjoy life, nor would markets thrive among them. Al-Hasan said: Forgetfulness and hope are two great blessings upon the children of Adam; were it not for them, the Muslims would not walk the roads. Al-Thawri said: It has reached me that man was created foolish, and were it not for that, life would not be enjoyable for him. Abu Sa‘id ibn ‘Abd al-Rahman said: The world is only inhabited due to the lack of intellect of its people. Salman al-Farsi, may Allah be pleased with him, said: Three things have amazed me until they made me laugh: one who hopes for the world while death pursues him; one heedless while heedlessness is not shown to him; and one laughing heartily while not knowing whether the Lord of the Worlds is angry with him or pleased. And three things have grieved me until they made me weep: parting from the beloved, Muhammad and his companions; the terror of the encounter; and standing before Allah, not knowing whether I will be commanded to Paradise or to the Fire. Some said: I saw Zurarah ibn Abi Awfa after his death in a dream and said: Which deed is most elevated among you? He said: Reliance on Allah and shortening hope. Al-Thawri said: Asceticism in the world is shortening hope; it is not eating coarse food nor wearing a woolen cloak. Al-Mufaddal ibn Fadalah asked his Lord to remove hope from him, and the desire for food and drink left him. Then he supplicated to his Lord, and hope was returned to him, and he returned to food and drink. Al-Hasan was told: O Abu Sa‘id, will you not wash your shirt? He said: The matter is more urgent than that. Al-Hasan said: Death is tied to your forelocks, and the world is folded behind you. Some said: I am like a man stretching his neck while the sword is upon it, waiting for when his neck will be struck. Dawud al-Ta’i said: If I hoped to live a month, you would see me commit grave sins. How can I hope for that when I see calamities befalling creation in the hours of night and day? It is related that Shaqiq al-Balkhi came to his teacher, called Abu Hashim al-Rumani, with something tied in the corner of his cloak. His teacher said: What is this with you? He said: Some raisins given to me by a brother, who said: I would like you to break your fast with them. He said: O Shaqiq, and you tell yourself that you will survive until night? I will never speak to you. So he closed the door in my face and entered. ‘Umar ibn ‘Abd al-‘Aziz said in his sermon: For every journey there is provision without fail, so take provision for your journey from this world to the Hereafter: piety. Be like one who witnesses what Allah has prepared of reward and punishment, so that you may desire and fear. Let not the span of time lengthen upon you, hardening your hearts and making you yield to your enemy. For by Allah, none extends hope except one who does not know—perhaps he will not reach the morning after his evening, nor the evening after his morning. And perhaps between them are the sudden snatchings of death. How many have I seen, and you have seen, who were deceived by the world? Only the eye of one who is confident of salvation from Allah’s punishment is content. Only one who is safe from the terrors of the Resurrection rejoices. As for one whose wound is not treated from one side except that he is wounded from another, how can he rejoice? I seek refuge in Allah from commanding you to what I do not forbid myself, lest my transaction be lost, my defect appear, and my poverty be exposed on a day when wealth and poverty are revealed and the scales are set up. You have been preoccupied with a matter that, were the stars preoccupied with it, they would be extinguished; were the mountains preoccupied with it, they would melt; were the earth preoccupied with it, it would split. Do you not know that there is no station between Paradise and the Fire, and that you are bound for one of them? A man wrote to his brother: To proceed: The world is a dream, the Hereafter is wakefulness, and death is the intermediary between them. We are in the fragments of dreams. Peace. Another wrote to his brother: Grief over the world is long, death is near to man, deficiency has a share in him every day, and calamity creeps in his body. So hasten before the call to depart is made. Peace. Al-Hasan said: Before Adam, peace be upon him, sinned, his hope was behind his back and his appointed time was before his eyes. When he committed the sin, it was reversed: his hope was placed before his eyes and his appointed time behind his back. ‘Abdullah ibn Samit said: I heard my father say: O you who are deceived by prolonged health, have you not seen anyone die without illness? O you who are deceived by long respite, have you not seen anyone taken without warning? If you reflected on the length of your life, you would forget the pleasures that have passed. Do you feel secure through health? Do you rejoice in prolonged well-being? Do you feel safe from death? Do you dare defy the Angel of Death? When the Angel of Death comes, your wealth will not prevent him, nor your numerous supporters. Do you not know that the hour of death is full of distress, choking, and regret over negligence? Then it is said: May Allah have mercy on a servant who worked for what is after death. May Allah have mercy on a servant who looked to himself before the descent of death. Abu Zakariyya al-Taymi said: While Sulayman ibn ‘Abd al-Malik was in the mosque...

When the forbidden was brought forth, a hollowed stone was presented, and someone was sought to read it. Wahb ibn Munabbih was brought, and within it was written: "O son of Adam, if you could see how near the remainder of your life is, you would renounce the length of your hopes, yearn to increase your good deeds, and shorten your greed and schemes. Tomorrow, only regret will meet you when your foot slips, your family and servants abandon you, your parent and kin depart, and your child and relative forsake you. You will not return to your worldly life, nor add to your good deeds. So work for the Day of Resurrection before regret and remorse." Sulayman wept bitterly.

Some said: I saw a letter from Muhammad ibn Yusuf to 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Yusuf: "Peace be upon you. I praise God to you, besides whom there is no deity. To proceed: I warn you of your departure from the abode of respite to the abode of your settlement and the recompense of your deeds. You will then reside in the depths of the earth after its surface. Munkar and Nakir will come to you, seat you, and rebuke you. If God is with you, there is no despair, loneliness, or need. But if it is otherwise, may God protect me and you from an evil fate and a narrow resting place. Then the cry of the Gathering and the blast of the Trumpet will reach you, and the Almighty will rise to judge between creatures, the earth emptied of its inhabitants and the heavens of their dwellers. Secrets will be disclosed, the Fire kindled, the Scales set, the Prophets and witnesses brought forth, judgment passed with truth, and it will be said: 'Praise be to God, Lord of the worlds.' How many will be exposed and concealed, how many lost and saved, how many tormented and shown mercy! Would that I knew my state and yours on that day. In this is what demolishes pleasures, consoles from desires, shortens hopes, awakens the sleepers, and warns the heedless. May God assist us and you in this immense peril, and place the world and the Hereafter in my heart and yours as they are in the hearts of the God-fearing, for we are by Him and for Him. Peace."

'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz delivered a sermon: He praised God and extolled Him, then said: "O people, you were not created in vain, nor will you be left aimless. You have a return where God will gather you for judgment and justice between you. Wretched and miserable tomorrow is the servant whom God excludes from His mercy, which encompasses all things, and from His Paradise, whose width is the heavens and the earth. Safety tomorrow is only for those who fear and are pious, who trade the little for the much, the perishable for the lasting, misery for happiness. Do you not see that you are in the garments of the perished, and after you the survivors will succeed? Do you not see that each day you escort one departing and returning to God Almighty, whose term has ended and hope severed, placing him in a crevice of the earth, neither cushioned nor smoothed, having cast off means, parted from loved ones, and faced the Reckoning? By God, I say this while knowing no one among you has more sins than I know of myself, but these are just laws from God, commanding obedience to Him and forbidding disobedience. I seek God's forgiveness." He placed his sleeve over his face and wept until his tears soaked his beard, and he did not return to his seat until he died.

Al-Qa'qa' ibn Hakim said: "I have prepared for death for thirty years; if it came to me, I would not wish to delay anything for anything."

Al-Thawri said: "I saw an elder in the mosque of Kufa saying: 'I have been in this mosque for thirty years as if awaiting death to descend upon me. If it came to me, I would not command it to do anything, nor forbid it from anything, nor have any claim over anyone, nor anyone over me.'"

'Abdullah ibn Tha'labah said: "You laugh while perhaps your shrouds have already left the washerman."

Abu Muhammad ibn 'Ali the ascetic said: "We went out for a funeral in Kufa, and Dawud al-Ta'i went out with it. He withdrew and sat aside while it was buried. I came and sat near him, and he spoke: 'Whoever fears the threat, the distant becomes near to him; whoever's hope is long, his deeds weaken; and everything to come is near. Know, O my brother, that everything distracting you from your Lord is an ill omen upon you. Know that all the people of the graves regret what they leave behind and rejoice in what they sent ahead. What the people of the graves regret, the people of the world fight over, compete for, and dispute before judges.'"

It is narrated that Ma'ruf al-Karkhi, may God have mercy on him, led the prayer. Muhammad ibn Abi Tubbah said: "He told me, 'Lead.' I said, 'If I lead you in this prayer, I will not lead you in another.' Ma'ruf said, 'And you imagine you will pray another prayer? We seek refuge in God from long hope, for it prevents good deeds.'"

'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz said in his sermon: "This world is not your abode of permanence; it is an abode for which God has decreed annihilation and decreed departure for its inhabitants. How many a well-established abode will soon be ruined, and how many a contented resident will soon depart! So improve, may God have mercy on you, your journey from it with the best of what you possess for the move, and take provision, for the best provision is piety. The world is but like the shade of a mirage that vanishes while the son of Adam is still competing in it."

He is the one whose eye is powerful when God calls him by His decree and casts him to the day of his demise, thus stripping him of his traces and his worldly life, and making his constructions and his abode for other people. Indeed, the world does not bring joy to the extent that it brings harm; it brings joy little and grieves long. And from Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, may God be pleased with him, that he used to say in his sermon: Where are those with handsome, beautiful faces, those who were proud of their youth? Where are the kings who built cities and fortified them with walls? Where are those who used to grant victory in the fields of war? Time has crumbled them, and they have become in the darkness of graves. Beware, beware, then salvation, salvation.

(Explanation of the Cause of Prolonged Hope and Its Cure)
Know that prolonged hope has two causes: one is ignorance and the other is love of the world. As for love of the world, it is that when one becomes intimate with it, its pleasures, delights, and attachments, leaving it becomes heavy upon his heart, so his heart refrains from thinking about death, which is the cause of leaving it. Whoever dislikes something pushes it away from himself, and man is infatuated with false hopes, constantly wishing for what agrees with his desire. What agrees with his desire is remaining in the world, so he continuously imagines it and estimates it within himself, and estimates the consequences of remaining and what he needs of wealth, family, home, friends, animals, and all worldly means. Thus his heart becomes devoted to this thought, preoccupied with it, so he is distracted from the remembrance of death and does not consider its nearness. If the matter of death and the need to prepare for it occurs to him in some circumstances, he procrastinates and promises himself, saying, "The days are before you until you grow old, then repent." And when he grows old, he says, "Until you become elderly." And when he becomes elderly, he says, "Until you finish building this house, developing this estate, or return from this journey, or finish arranging for this child and his provisions, and arranging a dwelling for him, or finish subduing this enemy who gloats over you." He keeps procrastinating and delaying, and he does not engage in any task except that he attaches to completing that task ten other tasks. And thus gradually he delays day after day, and one task leads him to another, indeed to many tasks, until death snatches him at a time he does not expect. Then his regret becomes prolonged, and most of the people of the Fire and their cries are from procrastination; they will say, "Woe to us from 'I will do it later.'" The procrastinator, the wretched one, does not know that what calls him to procrastination today is with him tomorrow, and it only increases in strength and firmness with the passage of time. He thinks that one immersed in the world and preserving it could ever have leisure—far from it! None is free from it except he who casts it away:
No one has ever fulfilled his need from it,
And no seeker reaches an end except to another need.
The origin of all these hopes is love of the world, intimacy with it, and heedlessness of the meaning of His saying, "Love whom you will, for you will leave him." As for ignorance, it is that man may rely on his youth and consider death remote despite youth. The wretched one does not reflect that if the elders of his town were counted, they would be fewer than a tenth of the town's men. They are few because death in youth is more frequent; for every elder who dies, a thousand children and youths die. He may consider death remote due to his health, and consider sudden death remote, not knowing that this is not far-fetched. Even if that is remote, sudden illness is not remote, and every illness occurs suddenly. When he falls ill, death is not remote. If this heedless one reflected and knew that death has no specific time—neither youth, old age, nor middle age; neither summer, winter, autumn, nor spring; neither night nor day—his awareness would be great, and he would busy himself with preparation for it. But ignorance of these matters and love of the world call him to prolonged hope and to heedlessness of estimating near death. He always thinks that death will be before him and does not consider its descent upon him and its occurrence to him. He always thinks that he escorts funerals and does not consider that his funeral will be escorted, because this has repeated for him and he is accustomed to it—witnessing the death of others. As for his own death, he is not accustomed to it and cannot imagine becoming accustomed to it, for it has not occurred. And when it occurs in another state after this, it is the first and the last. His way is to compare himself to others and know that inevitably his funeral will be carried and he will be buried in his grave. Perhaps the brick that will cover his grave has already been molded and finished, while he does not know. So his procrastination is pure ignorance. When you know that its cause is ignorance and love of the world, its cure is to remove its cause. As for ignorance, it is removed by pure thought from a present heart and by hearing profound wisdom from pure hearts. As for love of the world, the cure is to extract it from the heart, which is severe, for it is the chronic disease that has exhausted the first and the last in its treatment. There is no cure for it except faith in the Last Day and in what it contains of great punishment and abundant reward. Whenever certainty of that is attained, love of the world departs from his heart.

As for the world, love for the weighty matter is what erases from the heart love for the trivial. So when one sees the insignificance of the world and the preciousness of the Hereafter, he scorns to turn his attention to the world at all, even if he were given dominion over the earth from east to west. How could it be otherwise when he possesses of the world only a small, troubled, and vexing portion? How then could he rejoice in it or allow love for it to become firmly rooted in the heart alongside faith in the Hereafter? So we ask God, exalted is He, to show us the world as He showed it to the righteous among His servants. And there is no remedy for establishing the thought of death in the heart like looking upon those of one's peers and contemporaries who have died, and how death came upon them at a time they did not reckon. As for one who was prepared, he has attained a great triumph. But as for one who was deluded by long hope, he has incurred a manifest loss. Let a person look every hour at his limbs and members, and contemplate how they will inevitably be consumed by worms, and how his bones will crumble. Let him reflect that the worm begins with his right eye first, or his left. There is nothing on his body except that it is food for worms, and he has nothing from himself except knowledge and deeds sincerely for the sake of God, exalted is He. Likewise, let him reflect on what we will mention of the punishment of the grave, the questioning by Munkar and Nakir, and of the Gathering and the Resurrection, and the terrors of the Standing, and the sounding of the call on the Day of the Greatest Presentation. Such thoughts as these are what renew the remembrance of death upon his heart and call him to prepare for it.

(Explanation of the Ranks of People Regarding Length and Shortness of Hope)
Know that people vary in this. Among them are those who hope for survival and constantly desire it. God, exalted is He, says: "He would wish that he might be granted a life of a thousand years" (Quran 2:96). Among them are those who hope for survival until old age, which is the utmost lifespan he has witnessed and seen, and he is the one who loves the world intensely. The Messenger of God, peace be upon him, said: "The old man is a youth in his love for seeking the world, even if his collar-bones rattle from old age, except for those who are God-fearing, and few they are." Among them are those who hopes for a year, so he does not busy himself with arranging what is beyond it, nor does he anticipate his own existence in the coming year. Yet this one prepares in summer for winter and in winter for summer, so when he gathers what suffices him for his year, he occupies himself with worship. Among them are those who hopes for the duration of summer or winter, so he does not store up winter clothes in summer, nor summer clothes in winter. Among them are those whose hope reverts to a day and a night, so he prepares only for his day, but not for the morrow. Jesus, peace be upon him, said: "Do not be concerned with tomorrow's provision, for if tomorrow is from your appointed terms, your provisions will come to you with your appointed terms, and if it is not from your appointed terms, then do not be concerned for the appointed terms of others." Among them are those whose hope does not exceed an hour, as our Prophet, peace be upon him, said: "O servant of God, when you reach the morning, do not promise yourself the evening, and when you reach the evening, do not promise yourself the morning." Among them are those who does not even anticipate survival for an hour, as the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, would perform dry ablution despite the availability of water before an hour had passed, saying to 'Ali, "I will not reach it." Among them are those for whom death is before his eyes as if it is befalling him, so he awaits it. This is the person who prays the prayer of one bidding farewell. In this regard, what is reported from Mu'adh ibn Jabal, may God be pleased with him, when the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, asked him about the reality of his faith, and he said: "I took not a single step except that I thought I would not take another after it." And as reported from al-Aswad, who was an Abyssinian, that he would pray at night and glance right and left. Someone said to him, "What is this?" He said, "I am looking to see from which direction the Angel of Death will come to me." These are the ranks of people, and for each are degrees with God. It is not that one's hope is limited to a month, like one whose hope is a month and a day, but rather between them is a difference in degree with God, for God wrongs not even the weight of an atom (Quran 4:40). Then the effect of short hope becomes apparent in hastening to work. Every person claims he is short of hope, but he is lying. This only becomes apparent through his deeds. For he attends to means he may not need for a year, and this indicates the length of his hope. The sign of success is that death is before the eye, not forgotten for an hour. So let him prepare for the death that may come to him at any moment. If he lives until evening, he thanks God, exalted is He, for His obedience and rejoices that he did not waste his day, but rather took his full share from it and stored it for himself. Then he begins the same anew until morning, and so on when he reaches the morning. This is not facilitated except for one whose heart is empty of concern for tomorrow and what will be in it. Such a one, if he dies, is blessed and gains, and if he lives, he rejoices in good preparation and the sweetness of intimate discourse. So death for him is bliss, and life for him is increase. So let death be on your mind, O wretched one, for the journey is urging you on while you are heedless of yourself. Perhaps you have drawn near to the abode and traversed the distance. You will not be like this except by hastening to work, seizing every breath in which you are granted respite.

(Explanation of Hastening to Work and Warning Against the Affliction of Delay)

Know that one who has two absent brothers, expecting the arrival of one tomorrow and the other after a month or a year, does not prepare for the one arriving in a month or a year, but only prepares for the one whose arrival is expected tomorrow. Thus, preparation is a consequence of the proximity of expectation. Whoever expects death to come after a year, their heart becomes preoccupied with the duration and forgets what lies beyond it. Then each day passes while they await the full year, not subtracting the day that has passed, and this prevents them from hastening to good deeds forever, seeing ample time for themselves in that year and thus delaying action. As the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, said: "What does any of you expect from this world except overwhelming wealth, or forgotten poverty, or debilitating illness, or confining old age, or death that makes ready, or the False Messiah—for the False Messiah is the worst absent one awaited—or the Hour, and the Hour is more grievous and bitter." And Ibn Abbas said: The Prophet, peace be upon him, said to a man while admonishing him: "Take advantage of five before five: your youth before your old age, your health before your sickness, your wealth before your poverty, your free time before your preoccupation, and your life before your death." And he, peace be upon him, said: "Two blessings are taken for granted by many people: health and free time." Meaning, they do not seize them and only recognize their value when they are gone. And he, peace be upon him, said: "Whoever fears sets out at night, and whoever sets out at night reaches the destination. Indeed, God's commodity is precious; indeed, God's commodity is Paradise." He, peace be upon him, said: "The Tremor has come, followed by the Resurrection, and Death has come with what it contains." And whenever the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, sensed heedlessness or negligence among his companions, he would call out to them in a loud voice: "Death has come to you, fixed and inevitable, either with wretchedness or with happiness." And Abu Huraira said: The Messenger of God, peace be upon him, said: "I am the warner, Death is the raider, and the Hour is the appointment." And Ibn Umar said: The Messenger of God, peace be upon him, went out while the sun was at the tips of the palm branches, and he said: "What remains of this world is only like what remains of this day compared to what has passed of it." And he, peace be upon him, said: "The likeness of this world is like a garment torn from its beginning to its end, remaining hanging by a thread at its end, and soon that thread will snap." And Jabir said: Whenever the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, delivered a sermon and mentioned the Hour, his voice would rise and his cheeks would redden as if he were warning an army, saying: "Morning and evening have come upon you. I have been sent and the Hour are like these two," and he joined his two fingers. And Ibn Mas'ud, may God be pleased with him, said: The Messenger of God, peace be upon him, recited: "So whoever God wills to guide, He expands his breast to Islam." Then he said: "When light enters the heart, it expands." It was said: "O Messenger of God, is there a sign for that which can be recognized?" He said: "Yes: turning away from the abode of deception, turning to the abode of eternity, and preparing for death before it descends." And Al-Suddi said regarding: "He who created death and life to test which of you is best in deed." Meaning: which of you remembers death most, prepares for it best, and fears and is cautious of it most. And Hudhayfa said: "There is no morning or evening except that a caller calls out: 'O people, depart, depart!'" And the confirmation of that is His saying, exalted is He: "Indeed, it is one of the greatest warnings to mankind, for whoever among you wishes to advance or delay." Regarding death. And Suhaym, the freedman of Banu Tamim, said: "I sat with 'Amir ibn 'Abdullah while he was praying, and he made his prayer brief, then turned to me and said: 'Relieve me of your need, for I am hastening.' I said: 'What are you hastening to?' He said: 'The Angel of Death, may God have mercy on you.' So I left him, and he returned to his prayer." And Dawud al-Ta'i passed by, and a man asked him about a hadith, so he said: "Leave me, for I am only hastening the departure of my soul." 'Umar, may God be pleased with him, said: "Deliberateness in everything is good except in deeds for the Hereafter." And Al-Mundhir said: "I heard Malik ibn Dinar saying to himself: 'Woe to you, hasten before the command comes to you! Woe to you, hasten before the command comes to you!' until he repeated it sixty times, I hearing him without him seeing me." And Al-Hasan used to say in his admonition: "Hasten, hasten, for it is only breaths; if they are held back, your deeds by which you draw near to God, exalted is He, are cut off. May God have mercy on a person who looks at himself and weeps over the number of his sins." Then he recited this verse: "We only count for them a number." Meaning the breaths; the last of the count is the departure of your soul, the last of the count is parting from your family, the last of the count is your entry into your grave. And Abu Musa al-Ash'ari strove intensely before his death, so it was said to him: "If you held back or showed yourself some gentleness." He said: "When horses are released and near the end of their course, they expend all they have, and what remains of my life is less than that." He did not cease from that until he died, and he used to say to his wife: "Tighten your saddle, for there is no bridge over Hell." And a certain caliph said from his pulpit: "O servants of God, fear God as much as you are able, and be a people who, when called out to, awaken. Know that this world is not an abode for you, so exchange it and prepare for death, for it has overshadowed you. Depart, for it has become earnest with you. Indeed, its end is diminished by the moment and demolished by the hour."

Indeed, the brevity of life's span makes it worthy of heed, and an absent one who finds the two renewals—night and day—bringing him fresh opportunities is most deserving of swift return. And an arrival who attains either triumph or misery is worthy of the finest preparation. So the God-fearing, before his Lord, is he who counsels his own soul, advances his repentance, and overcomes his desire. For his term is concealed from him, his hope deceives him, and Satan is assigned to him, deluding him with repentance only to lead him to it, and beautifying sin for him so he may commit it, until his death assaults him when he is most heedless of it. And indeed, between any one of you and Paradise or Hell lies only death descending upon him. Oh, what regret for the heedless one, or that his life becomes a proof against him, and his days lead him to misery. May God make us and you among those whom no blessing makes arrogant, whom no sin hinders from obeying God, and upon whom no regret settles after death. Truly, He hears supplication, and in His hand is all good, ever doing what He wills.

Some exegetes said concerning His words, "You tempted yourselves"—meaning with desires and pleasures—"and you awaited"—meaning with repentance—"and you doubted"—meaning you wavered—"until God's command came"—meaning death—"and the deceiver deceived you concerning God"—meaning Satan. Al-Hasan said: Be patient and firm, for these are but few days, and you are a halted caravan; soon a man among you will be called and he will answer without turning back. So depart with the good you have at hand. Ibn Mas'ud said: There is not one of you who wakes in the morning except as a guest, and what he has is a loan; the guest departs, and the loan is returned. Abu 'Ubaydah al-Baji said: We visited al-Hasan during the illness from which he died, and he said: Welcome and greetings, may God grant you safety and settle us and you in the Abode of Permanence. This is a manifest goodness if you are patient, truthful, and God-fearing. So let not your share of this news, may God have mercy on you, be that you hear it with this ear and let it out from this ear. For whoever saw Muhammad (peace be upon him) saw him coming and going, not placing brick upon brick nor reed upon reed, but a banner was raised for him, so hasten, hasten! Salvation, salvation! Why do you delay? You have come, by the Lord of the Ka'bah, as if you and the matter are together. May God have mercy on a servant who made life a single life: he ate a morsel, wore worn garments, clung to the earth, strove in worship, wept over sin, fled from punishment, and sought mercy until his term came to him while he was upon that. 'Asim al-Ahwal said: Fudayl al-Raqqashi said to me while I was asking him: O this one, let not the multitude of people distract you from yourself, for the matter will come to you alone, not them. And do not say, "I will go here and there," so the day passes you by in nothingness, for the matter is recorded against you. And you have never seen anything more beautifully sought nor more swiftly attained than a fresh good deed for an old sin.

(Chapter Three: On the Agonies of Death and Its Severity, and What Conduct Is Recommended at That Time)

Know that if there were before the wretched servant no distress, terror, or torment except the agonies of death alone, it would be sufficient to embitter his life, cloud his joy, and separate him from his heedlessness and neglect. And it would be fitting for him to prolong his reflection and greatly increase his preparation, especially since he is at every moment on the verge of it, as some wise men said: A distress in another's hand—you know not when it will overcome you. Luqman said to his son: O my son, an affair you know not when it will meet you—prepare for it before it surprises you. And it is astonishing that if a person were in the greatest pleasures and the sweetest gatherings of amusement, and he expected a soldier to enter upon him and strike him five blows with a stick, his pleasure would be spoiled and his life ruined, while at every moment he is on the verge of the Angel of Death entering upon him with the agonies of departure, yet he is heedless of it. This has no cause except ignorance and delusion.

Know that the severity of pain in the agonies of death is not truly known except by one who has tasted it; and whoever has not tasted it knows it only by analogy to the pains he has experienced, or by inference from the states of people in the throes of death. As for the analogy that testifies to it: every limb without spirit feels no pain, but when spirit is in it, what perceives pain is the spirit. So whenever a limb is wounded or burned, the effect travels to the spirit, and the pain is felt according to what reaches the spirit. The painful agent is distributed over flesh, blood, and other parts, so only some of the pain reaches the spirit. But if there is a pain that directly affects the spirit itself and meets nothing else, how immense is that pain and how severe! The agony of death is an expression for a pain that descends upon the very spirit, engulfing all its parts until no part of the spirit, spread through the depths of the body, remains untouched by pain. If a thorn pricks him, the pain he feels flows only in a part of the spirit that meets that spot struck by the thorn. And the effect of burning is great only because parts of the fire penetrate all parts of the body...

A portion of the burnt limb remains, both outwardly and inwardly, except that the fire touches it, and the spiritual parts diffused throughout all portions of the flesh sense it. As for a wound, it only affects the specific place the iron touched; thus, the pain of the wound is unlike the pain of the fire. The pain of extraction assaults the very soul and pervades all its parts, for it is the extracted, the dragged out from every vein among the veins, every nerve among the nerves, every part among the parts, every joint among the joints, and from the root of every hair and skin from the parting of the hair to the foot. So do not ask about its anguish and pain, to the point that they have said: "Death has no severity like being struck by the sword, sawn with saws, or clipped with shears." For cutting the body with a sword only pains due to its connection to the soul, so how about when the agent directly involved is the soul itself? The one struck cries for help and screams due to the persistence of his strength in his heart and his tongue. The voice and cry of the dying person are cut off only from the intensity of his pain, because the anguish has overwhelmed him, risen over his heart, and reached every part of him, weakening every strength and enfeebling every limb, leaving him no power to call for help. As for the intellect, it is shrouded and confused; as for the tongue, it is struck dumb; as for the extremities, they are weakened. He would wish, if he were able, to find relief through groaning, screaming, and calling for help, but he is incapable of that. If any strength remains in him, you hear from him, at the extraction and pulling of the soul, a bellowing and gurgling from his throat and chest. His color changes and becomes livid until it is as if the dust, which is the origin of his nature, has appeared from him. Every vein is pulled out individually, so the pain is spread throughout his interior and exterior until the pupils rise to the tops of his eyelids, the lips contract, the tongue retracts to its root, the testicles rise to the top of their place, and his fingertips turn green. So do not ask about a body from which every vein among its veins is pulled! If the pulled one were a single vein, its pain would be immense, so how about when the pulled one is the pained soul itself, not from a single vein but from all the veins? Then each of his limbs dies gradually: his two feet grow cold first, then his two shins, then his two thighs. For every limb there is stupor after stupor and anguish after anguish until it reaches the throat. At that point, his gaze is cut off from the world and its people, the door of repentance is closed upon him, and regret and remorse encompass him. The Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him, said: "The repentance of the servant is accepted as long as the death rattle has not begun." Mujahid said concerning God's saying, exalted is He: "But repentance is not for those who continue to do evil deeds until, when death comes to one of them, he says, 'Indeed, I have now repented,'" that it means when he sees the [angelic] messengers. At that moment, the countenance of the Angel of Death becomes apparent to him, so do not ask about the taste of death's bitterness and its anguish at the succession of its stupors! For this reason, the Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him, used to say: "O God, make easy for Muhammad the agonies of death." People only fail to seek refuge from it and deem it insignificant due to their ignorance of it, for things before their occurrence are only perceived by the light of prophethood and sainthood. Therefore, the fear of death was immense among the prophets, peace be upon them, and the saints, to the point that Jesus, peace be upon him, said: "O company of disciples, pray to God, exalted is He, to make easy this stupor," meaning death, "for I fear death with a fear that has brought me, out of my fear of death, to the brink of death." It is related that a group from the Children of Israel passed by a graveyard, and some of them said to the others, "If you were to pray to God, exalted is He, to bring out for you from this graveyard a dead person so you could ask him..." They prayed to God, exalted is He, and there appeared to them a man who stood up, with the mark of prostration between his eyes, having emerged from one of the graves. He said, "O people, what do you want from me? I have tasted death fifty years ago, and the bitterness of death has not subsided from my heart." Aisha, may God be pleased with her, said, "I do not envy anyone for whom death is made easy after what I saw of the severity of the death of the Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him." It is related that he, peace and blessings be upon him, used to say: "O God, You take the soul from between the nerves, the marrow, and the fingertips. O God, so help me against death and make it easy for me." And from Al-Hasan: "The Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him, mentioned death, its choking, and its pain, and said, 'It is like three hundred blows with a sword.'" He, peace and blessings be upon him, was asked about death and its severity, and he said: "The easiest death is like a thorn in wool. Does the thorn come out of the wool except with some wool [clinging to it]?" He, peace and blessings be upon him, entered upon a sick person and then said: "I know what he encounters; there is no vein in him except that it pains from death in its own way." Ali, may God honor his countenance, used to urge fighting and say, "If you are not killed, you will die. By the One in whose hand is my soul, a thousand blows with the sword are easier for me than death upon a bed." Al-Awza'i said: "It has reached us that the deceased feels the pain of death until he is resurrected from his grave." Shaddad ibn Aws said: "Death is the most dreadful terror in this world and the next for the believer, and it is more severe than being sawn with saws, clipped with shears, and thrown into cauldrons. If the deceased were to be resurrected and inform..."

The people of this world derive no benefit from their life nor pleasure from their sleep through death. And from Zayd ibn Aslam, from his father, who said: When something remains for a believer from his ranks [in Paradise] which he has not attained by his deeds, the agony of death is intensified for him so that through its pangs and distress he may attain his rank in Paradise. And when a disbeliever has done a good deed, the [suffering of] death is not made light for him, so that he may receive the full reward for his good deed [in this world] and then proceed to the Fire. And from one of them, that he used to ask many of the sick, "How do you find death?" Then when he fell ill, it was said to him, "And you, how do you find it?" He said, "As if the heavens were pressing down upon the earth, and as if my soul were exiting through the eye of a needle."

And the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: "Sudden death is a mercy for the believer and a cause of regret for the wicked." And it is narrated from Makhul, from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: "If a single hair from the deceased were placed upon the inhabitants of the heavens and the earth, they would die by the permission of God, the Exalted, for in every hair is death, and death does not befall anything except that it dies." And it is narrated: "If a drop of the agony of death were placed upon all the mountains of the world, they would melt." And it is narrated that when Abraham, peace be upon him, died, God, the Exalted, said to him: "How did you find death, O my intimate friend?" He said: "Like a hot iron rod thrust into damp wool and then pulled out." He said: "But verily, We have made it light for you."

And it is narrated from Moses, peace be upon him, that when his spirit came to God, the Exalted, his Lord said to him: "O Moses, how did you find death?" He said: "I found my soul like a sparrow being roasted on a griddle; it does not die to find rest, nor does it escape to fly away." And it is narrated from him that he said: "I found my soul like a living sheep being skinned by the hand of the butcher."

And it is narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he had a cup of water near him at the time of death, and he would put his hand in the water, then wipe his face with it and say: "O God, make easy for me the agonies of death." And Fatimah, may God be pleased with her, said: "O, the distress of your distress, my father!" And he was saying: "There is no distress for your father after this day."

And 'Umar, may God be pleased with him, said to Ka'b al-Ahbar: "O Ka'b, tell us about death." He said: "Yes, O Commander of the Faithful. Verily, death is like a thorny branch inserted into a man's innards, with every thorn gripping a vein. Then a man of immense strength pulls it, taking what it takes and leaving what it leaves."

And the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: "Verily, the servant struggles with the distress of death and its agonies, and his joints bid farewell to one another, saying, 'Peace be upon you; you part from me and I part from you until the Day of Resurrection.'"

These are the agonies of death for the allies and beloved of God. So what is our state, we who are engrossed in sins, and upon us, along with the agonies of death, descend the remaining calamities? For the calamities of death are three. The first: the severity of the soul's extraction, as we have mentioned. The second calamity: beholding the form of the Angel of Death and the entry of terror and fear from him into the heart. For if the greatest of men in strength were to see the form in which he seizes the soul of the sinful servant, he could not bear the sight of it. It is narrated from Abraham, the intimate friend, peace be upon him, that he said to the Angel of Death: "Can you show me the form in which you seize the soul of the wicked?" He said: "You cannot bear that." He said: "Yes, I can." He said: "Then turn away from me." So he turned away from him, then glanced back, and there was a man, black, with hair standing on end, foul of odor, clothed in black, with flames and smoke issuing from his mouth and nostrils. Abraham, peace be upon him, fell unconscious. Then he regained consciousness, and the Angel of Death had returned to his original form. He said: "O Angel of Death, if the wicked encountered nothing at death but the sight of your face, it would be sufficient for him."

And Abu Hurayrah narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him: "David, peace be upon him, was a man of intense jealousy. When he would go out, he would lock the doors. One day he locked them and went out. His wife looked out and saw a man in the courtyard. She said, 'Who let this man in? If David comes, he will surely face trouble from him.' David came and saw him and said, 'Who are you?' He said, 'I am he who fears no kings, and no veil can prevent me.' He said, 'Then, by God, you are the Angel of Death.' And David, peace be upon him, fainted in his place."

And it is narrated that Jesus, peace be upon him, passed by a skull and struck it with his foot and said, "Speak, by the permission of God." It said, "O Spirit of God, I was the king of such-and-such a time. While I was seated in my kingdom upon my crown, with my soldiers and attendants around me upon my royal throne, the Angel of Death appeared to me. Every limb of mine fell apart separately, then my soul departed to him. Would that all those multitudes had been separation, and would that all that companionship had been solitude!" This is a calamity encountered by the disobedient, from which the obedient are spared. For the Prophets only recounted the mere agony of the extraction, not the terror experienced by one who beholds the form of the Angel of Death. Even if he were to see it in his dream one night, it would embitter the rest of his life, so how about seeing it in such a state? As for the obedient, he sees him in the most beautiful and finest form.

It is narrated from 'Ikrimah, from Ibn 'Abbas, that Abraham, peace be upon him, was a man of intense jealousy, and he had a house in which he worshipped.

Then he closed it upon leaving. One day he returned to find a man inside the house. He asked, "Who let you into my home?" The man replied, "The Lord of this house let me in." He said, "I am its lord." The man responded, "He who has greater authority over it than you or me let me in." He asked, "Which angel are you?" He said, "I am the Angel of Death." He said, "Can you show me the form in which you take the soul of a believer?" He said, "Yes, but turn away from me." So he turned away, then looked back and saw a youth—and he described the beauty of his face, the fineness of his clothes, and the fragrance of his scent. He said, "O Angel of Death, if a believer at death saw only your form, that would suffice him."

Among them is witnessing the two guardian angels. Wahb said: It has reached us that no one dies until his two recording angels appear to him with his deeds. If he was obedient, they say to him, "May God reward you well on our behalf. How many gatherings of truth you seated us in, and how many righteous deeds you brought us to!" If he was wicked, they say to him, "May God not reward you well on our behalf. How many evil gatherings you seated us in, and how many unrighteous deeds you brought us to, and foul speech you made us hear! May God not reward you well on our behalf." That is the fixing of the dying person’s gaze upon them, and he never returns to this world.

The third calamity: witnessing the places of the disobedient in the Fire, and their fear before actually seeing it. For in the throes of death their strength fails and their souls submit to departure, yet their souls will not depart until they hear the announcement of the Angel of Death in one of two forms: either "Rejoice, O enemy of God, with the Fire!" or "Rejoice, O friend of God, with Paradise!" From this arises the fear of the possessors of intellect. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: "None of you will leave this world until he knows his destination and sees his place in Paradise or the Fire." And he (peace be upon him) said: "Whoever loves to meet God, God loves to meet him; and whoever hates to meet God, God hates to meet him." They said, "But we all dislike death." He said, "It is not that. When a believer is shown what lies ahead, he loves to meet God and God loves to meet him."

It is narrated that Hudhayfah ibn al-Yaman said to Ibn Mas'ud, who was with him late at night, "Get up and see what hour it is." Ibn Mas'ud got up and then came to him and said, "The red dawn has appeared." Hudhayfah said, "I seek refuge in God from a morning that leads to the Fire." Marwan entered upon Abu Hurayrah, and Marwan said, "O God, make it light for him." Abu Hurayrah said, "O God, make it severe." Then Abu Hurayrah wept and said, "By God, I do not weep out of sorrow for this world nor from distress at parting from you, but I await one of the two announcements from my Lord: of Paradise or of the Fire."

It is narrated in a hadith from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: "When God is pleased with a servant, He says, 'O Angel of Death, go to so-and-so and bring me his soul that I may give him rest. I am sufficient regarding his deeds; I have tested him and found him as I love.' Then the Angel of Death descends with five hundred angels, carrying stalks of sweet basil and roots of saffron, each one bringing him glad tidings different from his companion's. The angels stand in rows for the departure of his soul, with them the sweet basil. When Iblis sees them, he places his hand on his head and shrieks. His soldiers say to him, 'What is wrong, our master?' He says, 'Do you not see the honor bestowed upon this servant? Where were you in this matter?' They say, 'We strove against him, but he was protected.'"

Al-Hasan said: "The believer finds no rest except in meeting God. And for him whose rest is in meeting God Almighty, the day of death is a day of joy, happiness, security, honor, and nobility." It was said to Jabir ibn Zayd at the time of death, "What do you desire?" He said, "A look at al-Hasan." When al-Hasan entered upon him, it was said, "This is al-Hasan." He raised his eyes to him and then said, "O my brothers, by God, this very hour I part from you, either to the Fire or to Paradise." Muhammad ibn Was'i said at the time of death, "O my brothers, peace be upon you—either to the Fire, or may God pardon." Some wished to remain in the agony of death forever and not be resurrected for reward or punishment. Thus, the fear of a bad ending has pierced the hearts of the knowers of God, and it is among the great calamities at death. We have mentioned the meaning of a bad ending and the intensity of the knowers' fear of it in the Book of Fear and Hope, which is fitting for this place, but we shall not prolong by repeating it.

(Explanation of What is Desirable from the State of the Dying Person at Death)
Know that what is beloved at death regarding the appearance of the dying person is calmness and stillness; regarding his tongue, that it be speaking the testimony of faith; and regarding his heart, that it have good hope in God Almighty. As for appearance, it is narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: "Observe the deceased at three signs: if his forehead perspires, his eyes shed tears, and his lips dry—that is from God's mercy that has descended upon him; and if he gasps like one being strangled, his color reddens, and his lips darken—that is from God's punishment that has descended upon him." As for his tongue being fluent with the word of testimony, it is a sign of good. Abu Sa'id al-Khudri said: The Messenger of God (peace be upon him) said: "Prompt your dying ones..."

Your deceased ones, "There is no god but Allah." In the narration of Hudhayfah: "For it demolishes what came before it of sins." And 'Uthman said: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: "Whoever dies while knowing that there is no god but Allah enters Paradise." And 'Ubaydullah testified to it. And 'Uthman said: When the dying person is in the throes of death, then prompt him with 'There is no god but Allah,' for there is no servant whose final words are sealed with it at his death except that it becomes his provision to Paradise. And 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, said: Be present with your dying ones and remind them, for they see what you do not see, and prompt them with 'There is no god but Allah.' And Abu Hurayrah said: I heard the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, say: "The Angel of Death attended a man who was dying. He looked into his heart and found nothing in it. So he opened his jaws and found the tip of his tongue stuck to his palate, saying 'There is no god but Allah,' so he was forgiven by the word of sincerity."

The one who prompts should not be insistent in the prompting but should be gentle, for perhaps the tongue of the sick person may not speak, and that would distress him and lead to him finding the prompting burdensome and disliking the word. It is feared that this might be a cause of a bad ending. The meaning of this word is that a man dies with nothing in his heart other than Allah. So when nothing remains for him as an object of desire except the One True Reality, his arrival by death to his Beloved is the utmost bliss in his regard. But if the heart is infatuated with the world, turned towards it, regretting its pleasures, and the word is merely on the tip of the tongue, and the heart does not accord with its realization, then the matter falls into the peril of the Divine Will. For the mere movement of the tongue is of little avail unless Allah, the Exalted, graciously accepts it.

As for having good expectations, it is recommended at this time, and we have mentioned that in the Book of Hope. Reports have come regarding the merit of having good expectations of Allah. Wa'ilah ibn al-Asqa' visited a sick man and said, "Inform me, what is your expectation of Allah?" He said, "My sins have drowned me, and I am on the brink of destruction, but I hope for the mercy of my Lord." Wa'ilah said "Allahu Akbar" and the people of the house said it with him, and he said, "Allah is Greatest! I heard the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, say: 'Allah, the Exalted, says: I am as My servant expects of Me, so let him expect of Me what he wills.'"

And the Prophet, peace be upon him, visited a youth who was dying and said, "How do you find yourself?" He said, "I hope in Allah and I fear my sins." He, peace be upon him, said, "These two do not gather in the heart of a servant in such a situation except that Allah gives him what he hopes for and secures him from what he fears." And Thabit al-Bunani said: There was a youth with some sharpness, and he had a mother who often admonished him and said to him, "O my son, you have a day (of reckoning), so remember your day." When the command of Allah, the Exalted, descended upon him, his mother leaned over him and began to say, "O my son, I used to warn you of this fate of yours and say, 'You have a day.'" He said, "O my mother, I have a Lord abundant in kindness, and I indeed hope that He will not deprive me today of some of His kindness." Thabit said: So Allah had mercy on him due to his good expectation of his Lord.

And Jabir ibn Waddah said: There was a youth with some levity who was in the throes of death. His mother said to him, "O my son, do you wish to bequeath anything?" He said, "Yes, my ring—do not strip it from me, for in it is the remembrance of Allah, the Exalted, so perhaps Allah will have mercy on me." When he was buried, he was seen in a dream, and he said, "Inform my mother that the word has benefited me and that Allah has forgiven me."
