And when the brother of Malik ibn Dinar died, Malik went out in his funeral procession weeping and saying, “By God, my eye will not be comforted until I know to what I have proceeded, and I will not know as long as I am alive.”

And al-A‘mash said, “We used to attend funerals and not know whom to console, for all were grieving.”

And Thabit al-Bunani said, “We used to attend funerals and see none but one weeping with his head covered. Such was their fear of death. And now we look at a group attending a funeral, and most of them are laughing and amusing themselves, speaking only of his inheritance and what he left for his heirs. His peers and relatives think only of the stratagem by which...”

Each person only reflects upon what God wills regarding his own funeral procession and his state when carried upon it. The sole cause of this heedlessness is the hardening of hearts through abundant sins and transgressions, until we have forgotten God Almighty, the Last Day, and the terrors that lie before us. Thus, we amuse ourselves, become heedless, and occupy ourselves with what does not concern us. We ask God Almighty for awakening from this heedlessness, for the best state of those present at funerals is their weeping over the deceased. Had they understood, they would weep for themselves, not for the deceased. Ibrahim al-Zayyat looked upon people invoking mercy for the deceased and said, "If you invoked mercy for yourselves, it would be better for you. He has been delivered from three terrors: the face of the Angel of Death, which he has seen; the bitterness of death, which he has tasted; and the fear of the final outcome, from which he is now secure."

Abu 'Amr ibn al-'Ala' said: I sat with Jarir while he was dictating poetry to his scribe. A funeral procession appeared, and he stopped and said, "By God, these funeral processions have turned my hair gray," and he began to recite:
      Funeral processions alarm us as they approach,
      Yet we amuse ourselves when they depart.
      Like the alarm of a flock at a wolf's den,
      When it disappears, they return to grazing.

Among the etiquettes of attending funerals are reflection, alertness, preparation, and walking ahead of it with an attitude of humility, as we have mentioned its manners and traditions in the section on jurisprudence. Among its etiquettes is to think well of the deceased, even if he was a sinner, and to think ill of oneself, even if one's outward appearance is righteous, for the final outcome is perilous and its reality is unknown. Therefore, it is related from 'Umar ibn Dhar that one of his neighbors who had been excessive against himself died, and many people avoided his funeral. He attended it and prayed over him. When he was lowered into his grave, he stood at his grave and said, "May God have mercy on you, O so-and-so. You spent your life in affirmation of God's Oneness and soiled your face in prostration. If they say he was a sinner and full of faults, then who among us is not a sinner and without faults?"

It is recounted that a man immersed in corruption died in an area of Basra, and his wife found no one to help her with his funeral, as none of his neighbors knew of it due to his great wickedness. She hired porters, carried him to the prayer ground, but no one prayed over him. She then carried him to the desert for burial. On a nearby hill was a great ascetic. He saw her as if waiting for the funeral, then intended to pray over it. The news spread in the town that the ascetic had descended to pray over so-and-so, so the townspeople came out. The ascetic prayed, and they prayed with him. People were astonished at the ascetic praying over him. He said, "I was told in a dream: 'Go down to such-and-such place; you will see a funeral with no one except a woman. Pray over him, for he is forgiven.'" This increased the people's astonishment. The ascetic summoned the wife and asked her about his condition and his conduct. She said, "As is known, his entire day was spent in the tavern, occupied with drinking wine." He said, "Look, do you know of any good deeds from him?" She said, "Yes, three things: Every day he would sober up from his drunkenness at dawn, change his clothes, perform ablution, and pray the dawn prayer in congregation, then return to the tavern and occupy himself with vice. Secondly, his house was never without one or two orphans, and his kindness to them was greater than his kindness to his own children, and he was intensely concerned for them. Thirdly, he would sober up during his drunkenness in the darkness of the night and weep, saying, 'O Lord, which corner of Hell do You wish to fill with this vile one?' meaning himself." The ascetic departed, his perplexity about the matter resolved.

From Sila ibn Ashim, when he buried a brother of his, he said at his grave:
      If you are saved from it, you are saved from a tremendous thing,
      Otherwise, I do not think you will be saved.

(Explanation of the state of the grave and their sayings at the graves)
Al-Dahhak said: A man asked, "O Messenger of God, who are the most ascetic of people?" He said, "He who does not forget the grave and decay, leaves the excess adornment of this world, prefers what lasts over what perishes, does not count tomorrow among his days, and counts himself among the people of the graves."

It was said to 'Ali, may God honor his face, "Why have you taken the graveyard as a neighbor?" He said, "I find them the best neighbors; I find them truthful neighbors who restrain tongues and remind of the Hereafter." The Messenger of God, peace be upon him, said, "I have seen no sight except that the grave is more dreadful than it." 'Umar ibn al-Khattab, may God be pleased with him, said, "We went out with the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, to the graveyards. He sat by a grave, and I was the closest of the people to him. He wept, and I wept, and they wept. He said, 'What makes you weep?' We said, 'We wept because of your weeping.' He said, 'This is the grave of my mother, Aminah bint Wahb. I asked my Lord's permission to visit her, and He permitted me. I asked His permission to seek forgiveness for her, but He refused me. I was overcome by the tenderness a child feels.'"

'Uthman ibn 'Affan, may God be pleased with him, when he stood at a grave, would weep until his beard became wet. He was asked about that, and it was said to him, "You remember Paradise and Hellfire and do not weep, yet you weep when you stand at a grave?"

I stood by a grave, and he said: I heard the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, say: "The grave is the first of the abodes of the Hereafter. If its occupant is saved from it, what comes after is easier than it. And if he is not saved from it, what comes after is more severe." It was said that 'Amr ibn al-'As looked at the graveyard, dismounted, and prayed two units of prayer. He was asked, "This is something you did not used to do." He replied, "I remembered the people of the graves and what has come between them and this, so I wished to draw nearer to God with them." And Mujahid said: "The first thing to speak to the son of Adam is his grave. It will say: I am the house of worms, the house of solitude, the house of estrangement, and the house of darkness. This is what I have prepared for you, so what have you prepared for me?" And Abu Dharr said: "Shall I not inform you of the day of my poverty? The day I am placed in my grave." And Abu al-Darda' would sit by the graves. He was asked about that, and he said: "I sit with a people who remind me of my ultimate return, and when I rise, they do not backbite me." And Ja'far ibn Muhammad would come to the graves at night and say: "O people of the graves, why is it that when I call you, you do not answer me?" Then he would say: "By God, they are prevented from answering me, and soon I shall be like them." Then he would turn to prayer until the dawn rose. And 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz said to some of his companions: "O so-and-so, I was sleepless last night, pondering the grave and its occupant. If you were to see the deceased after three days in his grave, you would be terrified by his proximity after long familiarity with him, and you would see a house where vermin crawl, pus flows, and worms penetrate, along with the changing of the air and the decay of the shrouds after fine appearance, pleasant scent, and clean garment." Then he gasped a gasp and fell unconscious. And Yazid al-Raqqashi would say: "O you who are buried in his pit, secluded in his grave in his solitude, comforted in the belly of the earth by his deeds—I wonder by which of your deeds you rejoiced and by which of your brothers you were gladdened." Then he would weep until his turban became wet, then say: "By God, he rejoiced in his righteous deeds and was gladdened, by God, by his brothers cooperating in obedience to God Almighty." And when he looked at the graves, he would bellow like an ox bellows. And Hatim al-Asamm said: "Whoever passes by the graveyards and does not reflect for himself and does not supplicate for them has betrayed himself and betrayed them." And Bakr al-'Abid said: "O my mother, would that you had been barren concerning me! Verily, your son has a long imprisonment in the grave, and after that, a departure from it." And Yahya ibn Mu'adh said: "O son of Adam, your Lord has called you to the Abode of Peace, so see from where you will answer Him. If you answer Him from your worldly life and busy yourself with the journey to Him, you will enter it. And if you answer Him from your grave, you will be barred from it." And al-Hasan ibn Salih, when he overlooked the graves, would say: "How fine your exteriors are! The calamities are only within you." And 'Ata' al-Sulami, when night fell upon him, would go out to the graveyard and say: "O people of the graves, you have died—O my death!—and you have witnessed your deeds—O my deeds!" Then he would say: "Tomorrow 'Ata' will be in the graves." And he would persist in that until morning. And Sufyan said: "Whoever increases in remembrance of the grave finds it a garden from the gardens of Paradise, and whoever is heedless of its remembrance finds it a pit from the pits of Hell." And al-Rabi' ibn Khuthaym had dug a grave in his house, and when he found hardness in his heart, he would enter it, lie down, and remain as long as God willed. Then he would say: " 'My Lord, send me back, that I might do righteousness in what I left behind,'" repeating it. Then he would reply to himself: "O Rabi', I have sent you back, so act." And Ahmad ibn Harb said: "The earth is amazed at a man who prepares his resting place and smooths his bed for sleep, and it says: 'O son of Adam, why do you not remember your long tribulation? There is nothing between me and you.'" And Maymun ibn Mihran said: "I went out with 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz to the graveyard. When he looked at the graves, he wept, then turned to me and said: 'O Maymun, these are the graves of my forefathers from Banu Umayyah, as if they never shared with the people of the world in their pleasures and their living. Do you not see them as slain, upon whom calamities have descended, decay has firmly taken hold, and vermin have found a resting place in their bodies?' Then he wept and said: 'By God, I do not know of anyone more blissful than he who has gone to these graves and is secure from the punishment of God.'" And Thabit al-Bunani said: "I entered the graveyards, and when I intended to leave them, I heard a voice saying: 'O Thabit, let not the silence of their inhabitants deceive you, for how many a distressed soul is within them.'" And it is narrated that Fatimah bint al-Husayn looked at the funeral procession of her husband, al-Hasan ibn al-Hasan, covered her face, and said:
    And they were a hope, then they became a calamity.
    Truly, those calamities have become great and immense.
And it is said that she pitched a tent over his grave and secluded herself there for a year. When the year passed, they removed the tent, and she entered Medina. They heard a voice from the direction of al-Baqi': "Have they found what they lost?" They heard from the other direction: "Rather, they have despaired, so they have turned back." And Abu Musa al-Tamimi said: "The wife of al-Farazdaq died, and the notables of Basra went out in her funeral procession, among them al-Hasan. Al-Hasan said to him: 'O Abu Firas, what have you prepared for this day?' He said: 'The testimony that there is no god but God, for sixty years.' When she was buried, al-Farazdaq stood by her grave and said:"

I fear what lies beyond the grave, if You do not pardon me,
More severe and narrower than the grave's burning and constriction.
When the Day of Resurrection comes to me, a harsh driver
And a forceful herder will drive forth Al-Farazdaq.
Truly disappointed is he among the children of Adam who walks
To the Fire, with a shackled collar, blue-faced.

And they recited regarding the people of the graves:
Stand by the graves and say upon their grounds:
Who among you is submerged in its darknesses?
And who among you is honored in its depths,
Having tasted the coolness of security from its terrors?
As for the stillness for the one with eyes, it is one;
Distinction in its degrees is not discerned.
If they answered you, they would inform you with tongues
That describe realities after their states.
As for the obedient, he is settled in a meadow,
Leading to whatever he wills of its spreading trees.
And the criminal transgressor therein is tossed
In a pit, taking refuge with its serpents,
And scorpions hasten to him, while his soul
Is in severe torment from their stings.

And Dawud al-Ta'i passed by a woman weeping over a grave, and she was saying:
I have lost life and not attained it,
If they have laid you in the grave, entombed.
So how and where shall I taste the flavor of slumber,
When you, with your right hand, have been made my pillow?
Then she said, "O my son, in which of your cheeks did the worm begin?" And Dawud was struck senseless on the spot and fell unconscious. And he said...

Malik ibn Dinar said: I passed by the cemetery and began to say:
I came to the graves and called out to them:
Where is the honored and where is the despised?
Where is the arrogant in his authority,
And where is the purified when he boasts?
He said: Then I was called from among them—I heard a voice but saw no person—and it said:
They have all perished, so no informer remains,
And they have all died, and news has died.
The daughters of the earth come and go,
Erasing the beauties of those forms.
So O you who ask about people who have passed,
Do you not have in what you see a lesson?
He said: So I returned weeping.

Verses found written upon graves:
Found written upon a grave:
The silent tombs converse with you, though they are mute,
And their inhabitants beneath the earth are hidden.
O gatherer of the world for other than a provision,
For whom do you gather the world when you will die?

And found upon another grave written:
O gainer, is not your span wide,
And your grave fortified on all sides, secure?
But what benefits the entombed the prosperity of his grave,
When within it his body crumbles?

And Ibn al-Sammak said: I passed by the graves and found upon a grave written:
My relatives pass by the sides of my grave,
As if my relatives did not know me.
The heirs divide my wealth,
And they do not fail to deny my debts.
They have taken their shares and lived on—
By God, how quickly they forgot me!

And found upon a grave written:
Indeed, the beloved among beloveds is snatched away;
No doorkeeper or guard can prevent death.
So how can you rejoice in the world and its pleasures,
O you upon whom speech and breath are counted?
You have become, O heedless one, immersed in deficiency,
While you were in your time immersed in pleasures.
Death does not mercy the ignorant for his delusion,
Nor the one from whom knowledge was acquired.
How many a silent one has death in a grave you stood by,
From answering—we are not silent regarding what is in it.
Your palace was once inhabited, having honor,
But your grave today among the tombs is effaced.

And found upon another grave written:
I stood by the beloved when their graves
Were aligned like racehorses.
Then when I wept and my tears overflowed,
My eyes saw among them my place.

And found upon a physician's grave written:
I said when a speaker said to me:
Luqman has gone to his resting place.
So where is what is described of his medicine,
And his skill in water with his touch?
Far be it! He cannot repel from another
What he could not repel from himself.

And found upon another grave written:
O people, I had a hope,
But my term cut me short from reaching it.
So let a man fear his Lord,
For whom work was possible in his life.
I am not alone in being transferred where you see;
Each will be transferred to his burden.

These are verses written upon graves due to the shortcoming of their inhabitants in taking heed before death. The insightful one is he who looks at others and sees his place among them, and so prepares to join them, and knows that they will not depart from their place until he catches up with them. And let him realize that if one day from the days of his life—which he is squandering—were offered to them, it would be more beloved to them than the entire world, because they have come to know the value of lifetimes and the realities of matters have been unveiled to them. Their regret is only for a day of life, that the negligent one might make up for his negligence and be saved from punishment, and that the divinely aided one might increase his rank and have it multiplied for him.

The reward, for they only recognized the value of a lifetime after its severance, so their regret was for a single hour of life. Yet you are capable of that very hour, and perhaps you are capable of its like, yet you are squandering it. So condition your soul to regret its waste when the matter passes beyond choice, if you do not seize your portion of your hour with promptness. Indeed, a righteous man said: "I saw a brother of mine in God in a dream-vision, and I said, 'O so-and-so, you have lived, praise be to God, Lord of the worlds.' He replied, 'To be able to say it—meaning 'Praise be to God, Lord of the worlds'—is more beloved to me than the world and all it contains.' Then he said, 'Did you not see where they were burying me? For so-and-so stood and prayed two rak'ahs. To be able to pray them is more beloved to me than the world and all it contains.'"

(Clarifying their statements upon the death of a child)
It is incumbent upon one whose child or close relative dies to regard their preceding him in death as if they were on a journey and the child arrived first at the country which is their destination and homeland. Then his grief is not excessive, knowing he will soon join them, with nothing between them but precedence and delay. Such is death, for its meaning is to precede to the homeland until the one who follows catches up. When one believes this, his anguish and sorrow diminish, especially since there has come regarding the death of a child a reward that consoles every afflicted person. The Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him, said: "For me to precede [to Paradise] with a miscarried child is more beloved to me than to leave behind a hundred horsemen, all fighting in the cause of God." He mentioned the miscarriage to alert by the lesser to the greater, for the reward is according to the child's place in the heart. Zayd ibn Aslam said: "A son of Dawud, peace be upon him, died, and he grieved for him intensely. He was asked, 'What was his worth to you?' He said, 'The earth filled with gold.' It was said to him, 'Then for you in the Hereafter is a reward like that.'" And the Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him, said: "No Muslim loses three of his children and is resigned to God's decree, except that they will be a protection for him from the Fire." A woman near the Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him, said, "Or two?" He said, "Or two." Let the parent sincerely supplicate for his child at death, for it is the most hopeful supplication and nearest to acceptance. Muhammad ibn Sulayman stood at his son's grave and said: "O God, I begin the day hoping in You for him and fearing You for him, so realize my hope and secure my fear." Abu Sinan stood at his son's grave and said: "O God, I have forgiven him what was due to me from him, so forgive him what is due to You from him, for You are more Generous and more Noble." A Bedouin stood at his son's grave and said: "O God, I have granted him what he fell short of in his duty to me, so grant him what he fell short of in obeying You." When Dharr ibn 'Umar ibn Dharr died, his father 'Umar ibn Dharr stood after placing him in the grave and said: "O Dharr, grief for you has distracted us from grieving over you. Would that I knew what you said and what was said to you!" Then he said: "O God, this is Dharr; You gave me enjoyment of him as long as You gave me enjoyment, and You fulfilled his term and his provision, and You did not wrong him. O God, I had enjoined upon him obedience to You and obedience to me. O God, whatever reward You promised me for my affliction, I have granted that to him, so grant me his punishment and do not punish him." He made the people weep. Then he said upon departing: "We have no want after you, O Dharr, and we have no need for any person alongside God. We have gone on and left you, and had we stayed, we would not have benefited you." A man looked at a woman in Basra and said, "I have never seen such freshness; that is only from lack of grief." She said, "O servant of God, I am in a grief in which no one shares with me." He said, "How so?" She said, "My husband slaughtered a sheep on the day of Eid al-Adha. I had two beautiful boys playing. The older one said to the other, 'Do you want me to show you how my father slaughtered the sheep?' He said, 'Yes.' So he took him and slaughtered him, and we only became aware when he was writhing in his blood. When the cries rose, the boy fled and took refuge in a mountain, where a wolf overtook him and ate him. His father went out searching for him and died of thirst from the intense heat." She said, "So time has clothed me as you see." Such calamities should be remembered at the death of children to console oneself from intense anguish, for there is no calamity except that one can imagine what is greater than it, and what God averts in every circumstance is the greater portion.

(Clarifying visiting graves, supplicating for the deceased, and related matters)
Visiting graves is recommended in general for remembrance and taking heed, and visiting the graves of the righteous is for seeking blessings along with taking heed. The Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him, had forbidden visiting graves, then later permitted it. It is narrated from 'Ali, may God be pleased with him, from the Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him, that he said: "I had forbidden you from visiting graves, so visit them, for they remind you of the Hereafter, but do not speak foolishly." The Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him, visited his mother's grave with a thousand helmeted men, and no day was seen with more weeping than that day. On that day he said, "I was given permission to visit, but not to seek forgiveness," as we mentioned before.

Umm al-Mu'minin 'A'ishah (may Allah be pleased with her) came one day from the graveyards, so I said, "O Mother of the Believers, from where have you come?" She said, "From the grave of my brother 'Abd al-Rahman." I said, "Did not the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forbid it?" She said, "Yes, then he commanded it." One should not cling to this to permit women to go out to the graveyards, for they often wail excessively at the heads of graves, so the good of their visitation does not outweigh its harm, nor are they free on the way from exposure and display, and these are grave matters. Visitation is a recommended practice, so how can that be tolerated for its sake? Indeed, there is no harm in a woman going out in shabby clothes that repel the eyes of men, and that is on the condition of restricting oneself to supplication and refraining from speaking at the head of the grave. Abu Dharr said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said, "Visit the graves, for they remind you of the Hereafter; and wash the dead, for handling a hollow body is an eloquent admonition; and pray over the funeral processions, perhaps that will grieve you, for the grieved one is in the shade of Allah." Ibn Abi Mulaykah said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said, "Visit your dead and greet them, for there is a lesson for you in them." From Nafi', that Ibn 'Umar would not pass by anyone's grave without stopping at it and greeting its occupant. From Ja'far ibn Muhammad, from his father, that Fatimah, the daughter of the Prophet (peace be upon him), used to visit the grave of her uncle Hamzah on certain days, and she would pray and weep at it. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said, "Whoever visits the grave of his parents or one of them every Friday, he is forgiven and recorded as dutiful." From Ibn Sirin, he said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said, "A man may die while his parents are alive and he is undutiful to them, then he supplicates to Allah for them after their death, so Allah records him among the dutiful." The Prophet (peace be upon him) said, "Whoever visits my grave, my intercession becomes incumbent for him." And he (peace be upon him) said, "Whoever visits me in Madinah seeking reward, I will be an intercessor and a witness for him on the Day of Resurrection." Ka'b al-Ahbar said: "No dawn breaks except that seventy thousand angels descend until they surround the grave, striking with their wings and sending blessings upon the Prophet (peace be upon him) until when evening comes, they ascend, and a similar number descend and do the same, until when the earth splits, he will emerge with seventy thousand angels, honoring him." The recommended practice in visiting graves is to stand with one's back to the Qiblah, facing the deceased with one's face, and to give greetings, and not to wipe the grave, nor touch it, nor kiss it, for that is from the practice of the Christians. Nafi' said: I saw Ibn 'Umar a hundred times or more come to the grave and say, "Peace be upon the Prophet, peace be upon Abu Bakr, peace be upon my father," and then depart. From Abu Umamah, he said: I saw Anas ibn Malik come to the grave of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and stand, and he raised his hands until I thought he had commenced prayer, then he greeted the Prophet (peace be upon him) and departed. 'A'ishah (may Allah be pleased with her) said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said, "There is no man who visits the grave of his brother and sits by it except that he finds comfort with him and a reply is given to him until he gets up." Sulayman ibn Suhaym said: I saw the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) in a dream, so I said, "O Messenger of Allah, these people who come to you and greet you, do you understand their greeting?" He said, "Yes, and I reply to them." Abu Hurayrah said: If a man passes by the grave of a man he knows and greets him, his greeting is returned to him and he is recognized; and if he passes by a grave he does not know and greets it, his greeting is returned. A man from the family of 'Asim al-Jahdari said: I saw 'Asim in my dream two years after his death, so I said, "Have you not died?" He said, "Yes." I said, "Where are you?" He said, "By Allah, I am in a meadow from the meadows of Paradise, I and a group of my companions. We gather every Friday night and its morning with Abu Bakr ibn 'Abdullah al-Muzani, and we meet to share your news." I said, "Your bodies or your spirits?" He said, "Far be it! The bodies have decayed; only the spirits meet." I said, "Do you know of our visiting you?" He said, "Yes, it is known to us on the eve of Friday and the whole day of Friday and the day of Saturday until sunrise." I said, "How is that, to the exclusion of all other days?" He said, "Because of the virtue and greatness of Friday." Muhammad ibn Was'i used to visit on Friday, so it was said to him, "If only you delayed until Monday." He said, "It reached me that the dead are informed of their visitors on Friday and the day before it and the day after it." Al-Dahhak said: "Whoever visits a grave before sunrise on Saturday, the deceased is informed of his visitation." It was said, "How is that?" He said, "Because of the status of Friday." Bishr ibn Mansur said: During the time of the plague, a man would frequent the cemetery to attend prayers over the funeral processions, and when evening came, he would stand at the gate of the graveyards and say, 'May Allah comfort your loneliness, have mercy on your estrangement, overlook your misdeeds, and accept your good deeds,' not exceeding these words. The man said: I spent one evening and returned to my family and did not come to the graveyards to supplicate as I used to supplicate. While I was asleep, a great many people came to me, so I said, "Who are you and what do you need?" They said, "We are the people of the graveyards." I said, "What has brought you?" They said, "You have accustomed us to a gift from you upon your departure to..."

You have destroyed us!" I said, "And what is it?" They said, "The supplications you used to make for us." I said, "Then I shall return to that," and I did not abandon it thereafter. And Bashar ibn Ghalib al-Najrani said: "I saw Rabi'ah al-'Adawiyyah, the worshipper, in my dream, and I used to supplicate for her frequently. She said to me, 'O Bashar ibn Ghalib, your gifts come to us upon a platter of light, covered with silk kerchiefs.' I said, 'How is that?' She said, 'And thus is the supplication of the living believers; when they supplicate for the dead and it is answered, that supplication is placed upon platters of light and covered with silk kerchiefs, then it is brought to the deceased and it is said to him, "This is a gift from so-and-so to you."'"

The Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him, said: "The deceased in his grave is none other than like a drowning person crying for help, awaiting a supplication to reach him from his father, or his brother, or a friend of his. When it reaches him, it is more beloved to him than the world and all that is in it. And verily, the gifts of the living to the dead are supplication and seeking forgiveness." And one of them said: "A brother of mine died and I saw him in a dream. I said, 'What was your state when you were placed in your grave?' He said, 'A visitor came to me with a brand of fire, and were it not that a supplicant supplicated for me, I would have thought he was about to strike me with it.'"

From this, it is recommended to prompt the deceased after burial and to supplicate for him. Sa'id ibn 'Abdullah al-Azdi said: "I witnessed Abu Umamah al-Bahili while he was in the throes of death, and he said, 'O Sa'id, when I die, do with me as the Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him, commanded us.' He said, 'When one of you dies and you have leveled the earth over him, then let one of you stand at the head of his grave and say, "O so-and-so, son of so-and-so," for he hears but does not reply. Then let him say, "O so-and-so, son of so-and-so," a second time, for he will sit up straight. Then let him say, "O so-and-so, son of so-and-so," a third time, for he will say, "Guide us, may God have mercy on you," but you will not hear. Then say to him, "Remember what you departed upon from the world: the testimony that there is no god but God and that Muhammad is the Messenger of God; and that you were pleased with God as your Lord, with Islam as your religion, with Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him, as your Prophet, and with the Qur'an as your guide." For then Munkar and Nakir will each draw back and say, "Let us go, what keeps us sitting with this one when his proof has been prompted?" And God, Mighty and Exalted, will be his advocate against them.'" A man said, "O Messenger of God, what if he does not know his mother's name?" He said, "Then let him trace his lineage back to Eve."

There is no harm in reciting the Qur'an over graves. It is narrated from 'Ali ibn Musa al-Haddad, who said: "I was with Ahmad ibn Hanbal at a funeral, and Muhammad ibn Qudamah al-Jawhari was with us. When the deceased was buried, a blind man came and recited at the grave. Ahmad said to him, 'O you, reciting at the grave is an innovation.' When we left the cemetery, Muhammad ibn Qudamah said to Ahmad, 'O Abu 'Abdullah, what do you say about Mubashshir ibn Isma'il al-Halabi?' He said, 'Trustworthy.' He said, 'Did you write anything from him?' He said, 'Yes.' He said, 'Mubashshir ibn Isma'il informed me from 'Abd al-Rahman ibn al-'Ala' ibn al-Lajlaj, from his father, that he instructed when he was buried that the opening of Surah al-Baqarah and its closing be recited at his head, and he said, "I heard Ibn 'Umar instructing that."' So Ahmad said to him, 'Then go back to the man and tell him to recite.'"

And Muhammad ibn Ahmad al-Marwazi said: "I heard Ahmad ibn Hanbal say, 'When you enter the cemeteries, recite the Opening of the Book, the two seeking-refuge chapters, and "Say: He is God, the One," and make the reward of that for the people of the graves, for it reaches them.'"

And Abu Qilabah said: "I came from Syria to Basrah and stopped at al-Khandaq. I performed ablution and prayed two rak'ahs at night, then I laid my head on a grave and slept. Then I awoke, and the occupant of the grave was complaining about me, saying, 'You have harmed me since tonight.' Then he said, 'You do not know, and we know, but we are incapable of action.' Then he said, 'The two rak'ahs you prayed are better than the world and all that is in it.' Then he said, 'May God reward the people of the world well on our behalf; convey peace to them, for indeed from their supplication there enters upon us light like mountains.'"

The purpose of visiting graves is, for the visitor, to take admonition from them, and for the visited, to benefit from his supplication. Therefore, the visitor should not neglect supplicating for himself and for the deceased, nor neglect taking admonition from him. He attains admonition by picturing in his heart the deceased—how his parts were dispersed, how he will be resurrected from his grave, and that he will soon join him.

As is narrated from Mutarrif ibn Abi Bakr al-Hudhali, who said: "There was an elderly woman from 'Abd al-Qays who was devout. When night came, she would gird herself and stand at the prayer niche, and when day came, she would go out to the graves. It reached me that she was reproached for her frequent coming to the cemeteries. She said, 'The hard heart, when it becomes callous, nothing softens it except the traces of decay. I come to the graves, and it is as if I see them having emerged from between their layers, and as if I see those dust-covered faces, and those changed bodies, and those greasy eyelids. What a sight it is! If the servants were to imbibe it into their hearts, they would not find its bitterness harsh for the souls, nor its severity destructive for the bodies.'"

Rather, one should bring to mind the image of the deceased as mentioned by 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz, where...

A jurist entered upon him and marveled at the alteration of his appearance due to his abundant striving and worship. He said to him, "O so-and-so, if you were to see me after three days, when I have been placed in my grave, and the eyeballs have come out and flowed down upon the cheeks, and the lips have shrunk back from the teeth, and pus has issued from the mouth, and the mouth has opened, and the belly has swollen so it rises to the chest, and the spine has come out from the anus, and worms and pus have come out from the nostrils—you would see something more astonishing than what you see now."

It is recommended to praise the deceased and that nothing but good be mentioned of them. ‘Ā’ishah, may God be pleased with her, said: The Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him, said, "When your companion dies, leave him and do not revile him." And he, peace and blessings be upon him, said, "Do not insult the dead, for they have proceeded to what they have sent forth." And he, peace and blessings be upon him, said, "Do not mention your dead except with good, for if they are among the people of Paradise, you will have sinned, and if they are among the people of the Fire, then what they are in is sufficient for them." And Anas ibn Mālik said: "A funeral passed by the Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him, and they praised it with evil. So he, upon him be peace, said, 'It has become incumbent.' And another passed by and they praised it with good. So the Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him, said, 'It has become incumbent.' ‘Umar asked him about that, and he said, 'This one, you praised him with good, so Paradise has become incumbent for him. And this one, you praised him with evil, so the Fire has become incumbent for him. And you are God's witnesses on earth.'" And Abū Hurayrah said: The Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him, said, "Indeed, a servant dies and the people praise him with praise, while God knows otherwise from him. So God, exalted is He, says to His angels, 'I call you to witness that I have accepted the testimony of My servants concerning My servant, and I have overlooked what I know of My servant.'"

(The Seventh Chapter: On the Reality of Death and What the Deceased Encounters in the Grave until the Blast of the Trumpet)
(Explanation of the Reality of Death)

Know that people have false assumptions concerning the reality of death in which they have erred. Some of them assumed that death is annihilation, and that there is no resurrection or gathering, and no consequence for good and evil, and that the death of a human is like the death of animals and the withering of plants. This is the view of the atheists and all who do not believe in God and the Last Day. A group assumed that he becomes annihilated by death and neither suffers harm nor enjoys reward as long as he is in the grave until he is restored at the time of the gathering. Others said that the spirit remains and is not annihilated by death, and that only the spirits are rewarded and punished, not the bodies, and that the bodies are not resurrected or gathered at all. All these are corrupt assumptions deviating from the truth. Rather, what the ways of reflection testify to and what the verses and reports declare is that the meaning of death is merely a change of state, and that the spirit remains after parting from the body, either in torment or in bliss. The meaning of its parting from the body is the cessation of its governance over the body due to the body's departure from its obedience. For the limbs are instruments of the spirit; it uses them such that it strikes with the hand, hears with the ear, sees with the eye, and knows the reality of things with the heart—and the heart here is an expression for the spirit. The spirit knows things by itself without an instrument. Therefore, it may suffer by itself with types of grief, sorrow, and anguish, and it may enjoy types of joy and delight, all of which are not connected to the limbs. Thus, whatever is a description of the spirit by itself remains with it after parting from the body, and whatever is for it by means of the limbs becomes inoperative with the death of the body until the spirit is returned to the body. It is not far-fetched that the spirit is returned to the body in the grave, nor is it far-fetched that it is delayed until the Day of Resurrection. God knows best what He has decreed for every servant of His servants. The body's becoming inoperative by death resembles the limbs of a living person becoming inoperative due to a corruption of temperament that occurs in it, or due to a severity that occurs in the nerves preventing the spirit's penetration into them. Thus, the knowing, reasoning, perceiving spirit remains, using some limbs while some have become refractory to it. Death is an expression for the refractoriness of all the limbs. All the limbs are instruments, and the spirit is the one that uses them. By "spirit" I mean the meaning within a human that perceives knowledge, the pains of sorrows, and the pleasures of joys. Whenever its governance over the limbs ceases, the knowledge and perceptions do not cease from it, nor do the joys and sorrows cease from it, nor does its receptivity to pains and pleasures cease from it. In reality, the human is the meaning that perceives knowledge, pains, and pleasures, and that does not die—that is, it is not annihilated. The meaning of death is the cessation of its governance over the body and the body's departure from being an instrument for it, just as the meaning of lameness is the hand's departure from being a usable instrument. Thus, death is absolute lameness in all the limbs, and the reality of the human is his self and his spirit, and it remains. Yes, his state changes in two respects: One is that his eye, his ear, his tongue, his hand, his foot, and all his limbs are taken from him; his family, his children, his relatives, and all his acquaintances are taken from him; his horses, his beasts, his servants, his houses, his estates, and all his possessions are taken from him. There is no difference between

That these things be stripped from man, and that man be stripped from these things—the pain lies in separation, and separation occurs sometimes by a man's wealth being plundered, and sometimes by a man being taken captive away from his kingdom and wealth. The pain is one in both cases. The meaning of death is but the stripping of man from his possessions by his removal to another world unsuited to this one. If he had in this world something he found solace in, took comfort from, and considered significant by its existence, then his grief over it after death becomes immense and his misery in parting from it severe. Indeed, his heart turns to each single item of his wealth, status, and property, even to a shirt he used to wear, for example, and he rejoices in it. But if he did not rejoice except in the remembrance of God and found solace only in Him, then his bliss is immense and his happiness complete when he is left alone with his Beloved and the obstacles and distractions are cut off from him, for all worldly causes are distractions from the remembrance of God. This is one aspect of the difference between the state of death and the state of life.

The second is that what was not unveiled to him in life is unveiled to him through death, just as what was not unveiled to him in sleep may be unveiled to the one who is awake. People are asleep; when they die, they awaken. The first thing unveiled to him is what harms him and benefits him from his good and bad deeds. That was inscribed in a book folded within the secret of his heart, and the distractions of the world preoccupied him from perceiving it. When the distractions are cut off, all his deeds are unveiled to him. He looks upon his evil deeds only to grieve over them with a grief that makes him wish to plunge into the depths of the Fire to be free from that regret. To such a servant it is said: "Enough is yourself this day as a reckoner against you." All this is unveiled at the severing of the breath and before burial, and the fires of separation—meaning separation from what he felt at peace with in this perishing world, not from what he desired from it for provision and sufficiency—ignite within him. For he who seeks provision for sufficiency, when he reaches the destination, rejoices at parting with the remainder of the provision, since he did not want the provision for its own sake. This is the state of one who took from the world only out of necessity and wished for his necessity to cease so he could be free of it; he has attained what he wished and is free of it.

These are types of great punishment and pains that assail him before burial. Then, at burial, his spirit may be returned to his body for another type of punishment, or he may be pardoned. The state of one who indulged in the world and felt at peace with it is like the state of one who indulged in the absence of a king, in his house, his kingdom, and his womenfolk, relying on the king being lenient with him or on the king not knowing what vile actions he committed. Then the king seized him suddenly and presented him with a scroll in which all his obscenities and crimes were recorded, particle by particle, step by step, while the king is overpowering, dominant, jealous for his sanctities, avenging against the criminals upon his kingdom, and not turning to anyone who intercedes for those who transgressed against him. Consider this seized one, how his state would be before the king's punishment descends upon him—of fear, shame, humiliation, grief, and regret. This is the state of the sinful deceased who was deceived by the world and felt at peace with it, before the punishment of the grave descends upon him, indeed at his death—we seek refuge in God from it. For disgrace, exposure, and the tearing of the veil are greater than any punishment that befalls the body from beating, cutting, and the like. This is an allusion to the state of the deceased at death, witnessed by the people of insight through an inward vision stronger than the vision of the eye, and testified to by evidences from the Book and the Sunnah.

Indeed, it is not possible to lift the veil from the essence of the reality of death, for he who does not know life does not know death, and knowledge of life is through knowledge of the reality of the soul in itself and the perception of its essential nature. "And it was not granted to the Messenger of God to speak concerning it, nor to add more than to say, 'The spirit is of the affair of my Lord.'" Therefore, it is not for any of the scholars of religion to disclose the secret of the spirit, even if they have perceived it. What is permitted is to mention the state of the spirit after death. That death is not an expression for the non-existence of the spirit and the cessation of its perception is indicated by many verses and reports. As for the verses, there is what came concerning the martyrs, as the Exalted said: "And do not think of those who are killed in the way of God as dead. Rather, they are alive with their Lord, receiving provision, rejoicing." And when the chiefs of Quraysh were killed at Badr, the Messenger of God called out to them, saying: "O so-and-so, O so-and-so, O so-and-so, I have found what my Lord promised me to be true. Have you found what your Lord promised you to be true?" It was said: "O Messenger of God, do you call out to them when they are dead?" He said: "By the One in whose hand is my soul, they hear this speech of mine better than you do, except that they cannot reply." This is a clear text regarding the spirit of the wretched one and the permanence of its perception and knowledge, and the verse is a clear text regarding the spirits of the martyrs. The deceased is not devoid of either happiness or wretchedness. And he said: "The grave is either a pit from the pits of the Fire, or a garden from the gardens of Paradise."

The Gardens of Paradise [110] This is an explicit text indicating that death means only a change of state, and that what will be of the deceased's misery or happiness is brought forward at the moment of death without delay, though some types of punishment and reward are deferred, but not their fundamental reality. Anas reported from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: "Death is the Resurrection; so whoever dies, his resurrection has come to pass." [111] And he (peace be upon him) said: "When one of you dies, his place is shown to him morning and evening. If he is among the people of Paradise, then from Paradise; and if he is among the people of the Fire, then from the Fire. And it is said: 'This is your place until you are resurrected to it on the Day of Resurrection.'" It is not hidden what is in the immediate witnessing of these places, of punishment or bliss. [112] And from Abu Qays, who said: We were with 'Alqamah at a funeral, and he said: "As for this one, his resurrection has come to pass." And 'Ali (may God honor his countenance) said: "It is forbidden for a soul to depart from the world until it knows whether it is among the people of Paradise or among the people of the Fire." And Abu Hurayrah said: The Messenger of God (peace be upon him) said: "Whoever dies as a stranger dies a martyr, is shielded from the trials of the grave, and is granted his provision from Paradise morning and evening." [113] And Masruq said: "I have never envied a believer in the grave who has found rest from the toil of the world and is secure from the punishment of God Almighty." And Ya'la ibn al-Walid said: I was walking one day with Abu al-Darda' and I said to him: "What do you wish for one you love?" He said: "Death." I said: "And if he does not die?" He said: "That his wealth and children diminish." And I only love death because none loves it except the believer, and death is the believer's release from prison. And I only love the diminishment of wealth and children because they are a trial and a cause for attachment to the world, and attachment to that from which one must inevitably part is the utmost misery. For everything other than God, His remembrance, and intimacy with Him must inevitably be parted from at death, without fail. For this reason, 'Abdullah ibn 'Amr said: "The likeness of the believer when his soul or spirit departs is like a man who spent the night in a prison and was then released from it, so he roams freely upon the earth and moves about in it." This which he mentioned is the state of one who has turned away from the world, grown weary of it, and had no intimacy except with the remembrance of God Almighty, and whom the preoccupations of the world held back from his Beloved, and the struggle with base desires pained him. So in death was his deliverance from all that causes pain, and his solitude with his Beloved, with Whom was his intimacy, without any hindrance or repeller. How deserving is that to be the ultimate bliss and delight! And the tasting of delights is for the martyrs who were killed in the path of God, for they only advanced to combat having severed their attachment to worldly ties, yearning for the meeting with God, content with being killed in seeking His pleasure. If he looks to the world, he has willingly sold it for the Hereafter, and the seller's heart does not turn back to what he sold. And if he looks to the Hereafter, he has purchased it and longed for it. So how immense is his joy with what he purchased when he sees it, and how little is his turning back to what he sold when he parts from it! The heart's purification for the love of God Almighty may occur in some states, but death does not overtake him in that state, so it changes. Combat is a cause of death, so it was a cause for death to overtake him in such a state. For this reason, his bliss is immense, for the meaning of bliss is that a person attains what he desires. God Almighty said: "And for them will be what they desire." [Qur'an] So this is the most comprehensive expression for the meanings of the delights of Paradise. And the greatest punishment is that a person is prevented from what he desires, as God Almighty said: "And a barrier will be placed between them and what they desire." [Qur'an] So this is the most comprehensive expression for the punishments of the people of Hell. And this bliss is attained by the martyr as his soul is severed, without delay. This is a matter revealed to the possessors of hearts by the light of certainty. And if you seek testimony for it from the realm of hearing, then all the narrations concerning the martyrs indicate it. Every narration that expresses the ultimate nature of their bliss with another expression... Indeed, it is reported from 'A'ishah (may God be pleased with her) that she said: The Messenger of God (peace be upon him) said to Jabir—and his father had been martyred on the day of Uhud—"Shall I not give you glad tidings, O Jabir?" He said: "Yes, may God give you glad tidings of good." He said: "Indeed, God Almighty has revived your father and seated him before Him and said: 'Ask of Me, My servant, whatever you wish, I will grant it to you.' He said: 'O Lord, I did not worship You as You deserve to be worshipped. I ask of You that You return me to the world so that I may fight alongside Your Prophet and be killed for You once again.' He said to him: 'It has already been decreed by Me that you shall not return to it.'" [114] And Ka'b said: A man will be found in Paradise weeping, and it will be said to him: "Why do you weep when you are in Paradise?" He said: "I weep because I was only killed once for God, and I wished to be returned so I could be killed for Him many times." And know that for the believer, following death, is unveiled from the vastness of God's majesty such that the world in comparison to it is like a prison and a constricted place. His likeness is like one imprisoned in a dark house, for whom a door is opened to a vast garden, whose furthest bounds cannot be reached, containing all kinds of trees, flowers, fruits, and birds. He would not desire to return to the dark prison. And the Messenger (peace be upon him) gave an example...

God gave an example, saying regarding a man who died: "This one has departed from the world at dawn, leaving it for its people. If he was content, he would not be pleased to return to the world, just as none of you would be pleased to return to his mother's womb." Thus, He made known to you through this that the vastness of the Hereafter compared to this world is like the vastness of this world compared to the darkness of the womb. And He said: "The likeness of the believer in this world is that of a fetus in its mother's womb; when it emerges from her womb, it cries over its exit until it sees the light and is placed down, then it does not desire to return to its place." Similarly, the believer is distressed by death, but when he reaches his Lord, he does not desire to return to the world, just as the fetus does not desire to return to its mother's womb. It was said to the Messenger of God: "So-and-so has died." He said: "Is he relieved, or are others relieved of him?" He indicated by "relieved" the believer, and by "others relieved of him" the wicked, for the people of the world are relieved of him. Abu 'Umar, the companion of the watering-place, passed by us when we were boys, and he looked at a grave where a skull was exposed. He ordered a man to bury it, then said: "These bodies are not harmed by this dust at all; it is the souls that are punished and rewarded until the Day of Resurrection." From 'Amr ibn Dinar: "No one dies except that he knows what will happen to his family after him. They wash him and shroud him, and he looks upon them." Malik ibn Anas said: "It reached me that the souls of the believers are set free to go wherever they wish." Al-Nu'man ibn Bashir said: "I heard the Messenger of God on the pulpit saying: 'Verily, nothing remains of this world except like a fly buzzing in its air. So fear God, fear God regarding your brothers among the people of the graves, for your deeds are presented to them.'" Abu Hurayrah said: The Prophet said: "Do not disgrace your dead with the evils of your deeds, for they are presented to your loved ones among the people of the graves." Because of this, Abu al-Darda' said: "O God, I seek refuge in You from performing a deed that would bring me shame before 'Abdullah ibn Rawahah"—and he had died, being his uncle. 'Abdullah ibn 'Amr ibn al-'As was asked about the souls of the believers when they die: where are they? He said: "In the crops of white birds in the shade of the Throne, and the souls of the disbelievers are in the seventh earth." Abu Sa'id al-Khudri said: I heard the Messenger of God say: "Verily, the deceased recognizes the one who washes him, the one who carries him, and the one who lowers him into his grave." Salih al-Murri said: "It reached me that the souls meet at death, and the souls of the dead say to the soul that comes out to them: 'How was your abode, and in which of the two bodies were you—in a good one or a bad one?'" 'Ubayd ibn 'Umayr said: "The people of the graves await news. When the deceased comes to them, they say: 'What did so-and-so do?' He says: 'Has he not come to you, or has he not arrived before you?' They say: 'To God we belong and to Him we return. He has taken a path other than ours.'" From Ja'far ibn Sa'id: "When a man dies, his children receive him as one receives an absent person." Mujahid said: "A man is indeed given glad tidings of his child's righteousness in his grave." Abu Ayyub al-Ansari narrated from the Prophet that he said: "When the soul of a believer is taken, the people of mercy from God receive it as a bearer of glad tidings is received in this world. They say: 'Look at your brother until he rests, for he was in severe distress.' They ask him: 'What did so-and-so do, and what did so-and-so do, and did so-and-so marry?' If they ask him about a man who died before him and he says, 'He died before me,' they say: 'To God we belong and to Him we return. He has been taken to his mother, the abyss.'"

(Explanation of the Grave's Address to the Deceased)

The speech of the dead is either by the tongue of statement or by the tongue of state, which is more eloquent in making the dead understand than the tongue of statement is in making the living understand. The Messenger of God said: "The grave says to the deceased when he is placed in it, and it calls out: 'O son of Adam, what deluded you about me? Did you not know that I am the abode of trials, the abode of darkness, the abode of solitude, and the abode of worms? What deluded you about me when you used to pass by me?' Then, if he was righteous, a responder answers on his behalf, saying: 'What do you think if he used to enjoin good and forbid evil?' The grave says: 'Then I am transformed for him into greenery, his body returns as light, and his spirit ascends to God Almighty.'" And "fadhadh" is the one who puts one foot forward and delays the other; thus the narrator explained it. 'Ubayd ibn 'Umayr al-Laythi said: "No one dies except that his grave, in which he is buried, calls out to him: 'I am the abode of darkness, solitude, and isolation. If you were obedient to God in your life, I am a mercy upon you today; but if you were disobedient, then I am a punishment upon you today. I am the one whom, if an obedient person enters me, he leaves delighted, and if a disobedient person enters me, he leaves destroyed.'" Muhammad ibn Subayh said: "It reached us that when a man is placed in his grave and is tormented or afflicted with something he dislikes, his neighbors among the dead call out to him: 'O you who remained behind in the world after your brothers and neighbors, did you not have...'"

There is a lesson for us in this. Did you not reflect upon those who preceded you? Did you not see the cessation of our deeds from us while you are in the respite? Why did you not recover what your brothers have missed? And you call out across the expanse of the earth, "O you who are deluded by the outward appearance of this world! Why did you not take a lesson from those of your family who have vanished into the earth's belly, those whom the world deceived before you, then their appointed time preceded them to the graves? And you see him being carried, his loved ones bearing him gently to the abode from which there is no escape."

Yazid al-Raqqashi said: It reached me that when the deceased is placed in his grave, his deeds surround him, then God gives them speech. They say, "O servant, alone in his pit! Companions and family have been cut off from you, so you have no intimate friend with us today."

Ka'b said: When the righteous servant is placed in the grave, his righteous deeds surround him: prayer, fasting, pilgrimage, striving, and charity. Then the angels of punishment come from the direction of his feet, and Prayer says, "You have no way to him, for he stood long for God upon them." They come to him from the direction of his head, and Fasting says, "You have no way to him, for he endured thirst for God in the worldly abode, so you have no way to him." They come to him from the direction of his body, and Pilgrimage and Striving say, "You have no way to him, for he exerted his soul and fatigued his body, performing pilgrimage and striving for God." They come to him from the direction of his hands, and Charity says, "Hold back from my companion! How much charity issued from these two hands until it fell into the hand of God, seeking His Countenance. You have no way to him." It is said to him, "Congratulations! You were wholesome in life and wholesome in death." Then the angels of mercy come to him, spread for him a bed from Paradise and a covering from Paradise, widen his grave as far as his sight reaches, and bring a lamp from Paradise by whose light he is illuminated until the day God resurrects him from his grave.

Abdullah ibn 'Ubayd ibn 'Umayr said at a funeral: It reached me that the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, said: "Verily, the deceased sits up and hears the footsteps of those escorting him. Nothing speaks to him except his grave, and it says, 'Woe to you, O son of Adam! Did I not warn you? Did I not warn you of my narrowness, my decay, my horror, and my worms? So what have you prepared for me?'"

(Explanation of the Punishment of the Grave and the Questioning by Munkar and Nakir)

Al-Bara' ibn 'Azib said: We went out with the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, for the funeral of a man from the Ansar. The Messenger of God, peace be upon him, sat at his grave, his head bowed, then said, "O God, I seek refuge in You from the punishment of the grave," three times. Then he said: "When the believer is in the throes of the Hereafter and the severance from the world, God sends down angels whose faces are like the sun, with his embalming perfume and shroud. They sit as far as his sight reaches. When his soul departs, every angel between heaven and earth prays upon him, and every angel in heaven, and the gates of heaven are opened. There is no gate but that it loves for his soul to enter through it. When his soul ascends, it is said, 'O Lord, Your servant so-and-so.' He says, 'Return him, and show him what I have prepared for him of honor, for I promised him, "From it We created you and into it We will return you and from it We will bring you forth another time."' And verily, he hears the shuffling of their sandals when they turn away, until it is said to him, 'O you, who is your Lord, and what is your religion, and who is your prophet?' He says, 'My Lord is God, my religion is Islam, and my prophet is Muhammad,' peace be upon him. He is then rebuked with a severe rebuke, and this is the last trial presented to the deceased. When he says that, a caller calls out, 'He has spoken the truth.' This is the meaning of His saying, exalted is He, 'God keeps firm those who believe with the firm word.' Then one comes to him, beautiful of face, fragrant of scent, fine of garment, and says, 'Receive glad tidings of your Lord's mercy and gardens wherein is everlasting bliss.' He says, 'And you, may God give you glad tidings of good. Who are you?' He says, 'I am your righteous deed. By God, I only knew you to be swift to God's obedience and slow to His disobedience, so may God reward you with good.' Then a caller calls out, 'Spread for him from the bedding of Paradise and open for him a gate to Paradise.' So bedding from Paradise is spread for him and a gate to Paradise is opened for him, and he says, 'O God, hasten the establishment of the Hour so that I may return to my family and my wealth.'

As for the disbeliever, when he is in the throes of the Hereafter and severed from the world, angels descend to him, harsh and severe, with garments of fire and shirts of pitch, and they surround him. When his soul departs, every angel between heaven and earth curses him, and every angel in heaven, and the gates of heaven are closed. There is no gate but that it hates for his soul to enter through it. When his soul ascends, it is cast away, and it is said, 'O Lord, Your servant so-and-so.' Heaven does not accept him, nor does earth. God, mighty and majestic, says, 'Return him, and show him what I have prepared for him of evil, for I promised him, "From it We created you and into it We will return you and from it We will bring you forth another time."' And verily, he hears the shuffling of their sandals when they turn away, until it is said to him, 'O you, who is your Lord, and who is your prophet, and what is your religion?' He says, 'I do not know.' It is said, 'You did not know.' Then one comes to him, ugly of face, foul of scent, vile of garment, and says, 'Receive tidings of wrath from God and of punishment...'

He will be in lasting torment, and it will be said: "May God give you evil for your evil. Who are you?" It will reply: "I am your wicked deed." And by God, you were swift in disobeying God and slow in obeying Him, so may God reward you with evil." And he will say to it: "And you, may God reward you with evil." Then a blind, deaf, and dumb being is appointed for him, holding an iron mace—if the two weighty kinds (of jinn and mankind) were to gather to lift it, they could not. If it were to strike a mountain, it would turn to dust. He strikes him with it once, and he becomes dust. Then the soul is returned to him, and he strikes him between the eyes with a blow heard by all on the earths, not just the two weighty kinds. Then a caller will call out: "Spread for him two slabs of fire and open for him a door to the Fire." So two slabs of fire are spread for him, and a door to the Fire is opened for him.

Muhammad ibn Ali said: "No one dies but that his good deeds and his evil deeds are presented to him at death." He said: "He gazes at his good deeds and turns away from his evil deeds." Abu Huraira reported that the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, said: "When a believer is near death, the angels come to him with a silk cloth containing musk and bundles of sweet basil. They draw out his soul as gently as a hair is drawn from dough, and it is said: 'O contented soul, come forth pleased and pleasing, to the spirit and grace of God.' When his soul is taken out, it is placed upon that musk and basil, wrapped in the silk, and sent up to the Highest Heaven. But when a disbeliever is near death, the angels come to him with a coarse cloth containing a burning ember. They wrench his soul out violently, and it is said: 'O wicked soul, come forth displeased and displeasing, to the disgrace and punishment of God.' When his soul is taken out, it is placed upon that ember—and it has a sizzling sound—wrapped in the coarse cloth, and taken down to the lowest depths."

From Muhammad ibn Ka'b al-Qurazi, regarding God's saying: "Until, when death comes to one of them, he says, 'My Lord, send me back, that I might do righteousness in what I left behind,'" he said: "What do you desire? In what do you wish? Do you wish to return to gather wealth, plant trees, build buildings, and dig canals?" He says: "No, that I might do righteousness in what I left behind." The Almighty says: "No, it is but a word he is saying"—meaning he will surely say it at death.

Abu Huraira reported that the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: "The believer in his grave is in a verdant meadow, and his grave is widened for him seventy cubits, and it is illuminated until it is like the full moon. Do you know why 'He will have a life of hardship' was revealed?" They said: "God and His Messenger know best." He said: "The punishment of the disbeliever in his grave: ninety-nine dragons are set upon him. Do you know what a dragon is? Ninety-nine serpents, each with nine heads, they scratch him, lick him, and blow into his body until the Day they are resurrected."

One should not be astonished at this specific number, for the numbers of these serpents and scorpions correspond to the number of blameworthy traits such as arrogance, ostentation, envy, rancor, malice, and all other attributes. They have countable roots, then branches diverge from them, also countable, then their branches divide into sections. Those very traits are the destroyers, and those very things transform into scorpions and serpents. The strong among them stings like a dragon, the weak stings like a scorpion, and what is between them harms like a serpent. The possessors of hearts and insight witness, by the light of insight, these destroyers and the branching paths of their offshoots, though the precise measure of their number is only known by the light of Prophethood. Such reports have sound outward meanings and hidden secrets, but they are clear to the possessors of insight. For one whose inner realities are not unveiled, he should not deny their outward meanings; rather, the lowest degree of faith is belief and acceptance.

If you say: "We observe the disbeliever in his grave for a period and watch him, and we do not see any of that. So how can belief be reconciled with contrary observation?" Know that you have three stations in believing such matters. The first, which is the most apparent, correct, and safest, is to believe that they exist and they sting the deceased, but you do not witness it. For this eye is not fit to perceive matters of the unseen realm, and all that pertains to the Hereafter is from the knowledge of the unseen. Do you not see how the Companions, may God be pleased with them, believed in the descent of Gabriel without seeing him, and believed that the Prophet, peace be upon him, saw him? If you do not believe this, then establishing the foundation of faith in angels and revelation is more important for you. And if you have believed it and allowed that the Prophet could see what the community did not see, then how can you not allow this for the deceased? And just as an angel does not resemble humans or animals, the serpents and scorpions that sting in the grave are not of the kind in our world; rather, they are another kind, perceived by another sense.

The second station is to recall the state of the sleeper, for he may see in his dream a serpent stinging him, and he suffers pain from it until you see him tossing in his sleep and his forehead sweating, and he may be startled from...

His place—all of this he perceives within himself and is pained by it, just as one awake is pained while witnessing it. And you see his outward form at rest, yet you do not see the serpent around him, though the serpent exists in his reality, and the torment is present, but in your perception it is not witnessed. And if the torment lies in the pain of a bite, there is no difference between an imagined serpent and one beheld.

The third station: You know that the serpent itself does not cause pain; rather, what afflicts you from it is its venom. Then the venom is not the pain itself, but your torment lies in the effect produced in you by the venom. If such an effect were to occur without venom, the torment would still be complete, and that type of torment could only be defined by attributing it to the cause that ordinarily leads to it. For if, for example, pleasure of intercourse were created in a person without the actual occurrence of its form, it could not be defined except by reference to it, so that the attribution serves to define it by its cause, while the fruit of the cause is attained even if the form of the cause is not. The cause is sought for its fruit, not for its own essence. These destructive attributes turn into harmful and painful afflictions in the soul at death, so that their pains become like the pains of serpent bites without the existence of serpents. And the transformation of an attribute into something harmful resembles the transformation of love into something painful at the death of the beloved. For if it was pleasurable, then a state supervenes whereby the pleasure itself becomes painful, such that it returns to the heart with kinds of torment through which one wishes he had never enjoyed the love and union. Indeed, this very thing is one of the types of torment of the deceased, for he subjected his soul to love in this world, so that he came to love his wealth, his estate, his status, his children, his relatives, and his acquaintances. If all of that were taken from him in his lifetime by one from whom he had no hope of recovery, what do you suppose his state would be? Would not his misery be immense, his torment severe, and would he not wish and say, "If only I had never possessed any wealth or status, so that I would not be pained by its separation"? Death is but an expression for the separation from all worldly beloved things at once:

What is the state of one who had a single beloved, and that single one was taken from him?

Then what is the state of one who finds joy only in the world, and the world is taken from him and delivered to his enemies? Then added to this torment is his regret for what he missed of the bliss of the Hereafter and the veil from God, Mighty and Majestic. For love of other than God veils him from meeting God and enjoying Him. Thus, upon him descends the pain of separation from all his beloved things and his regret for what he missed of the bliss of the Hereafter for all eternity, and the humiliation of rejection and the veil from God, Exalted is He. That is the torment with which he is punished, for nothing follows the fire of separation except the Fire of Hell, as He, Exalted is He, said: "No indeed! On that Day they will be veiled from their Lord. Then indeed they will enter the Blazing Fire."

As for one who did not find comfort in the world and loved none but God, and who yearned for the meeting with God, he has been delivered from the prison of the world and the struggle with desires within it, and he has advanced to his Beloved, and the obstacles and distractions have been cut off from him, and bliss has been made complete for him, with security from disappearance for all eternity. For the like of this let the workers strive.

The intended meaning is that a man may love his horse to such an extent that, if given the choice between it being taken from him and being stung by a scorpion, he would prefer to endure the scorpion's sting. Thus, the pain of separation from the horse is greater for him than that of the scorpion, and his love for the horse is what stings him when his horse is taken from him. So let him prepare for these stings, for death will take from him his horse, his mount, his house, his estate, his family, his children, his loved ones, and his acquaintances. It will take from him his status and his acceptance; indeed, it will take from him his hearing, his sight, and his limbs, and he will despair of all of that ever returning to him. So if he loved nothing else, and all of that is taken from him, that is greater upon him than scorpions and serpents. And just as if that were taken from him while alive his punishment would be immense, so too when he dies—for we have clarified that the essence which perceives pains and pleasures does not die; rather, his torment after death is more severe, because in life he consoles himself with means that occupy his senses, such as companionship and conversation, and he consoles himself with the hope of its return and the hope of compensation for it. But there is no consolation after death, for the paths of consolation have been closed to him and despair has set in.

Therefore, every shirt and handkerchief he loved, such that it would grieve him if taken from him, remains a source of regret and torment for him. If it was light in the world, he is safe—and that is the meaning of their saying, "The lightly burdened are saved." But if he was heavily burdened, his torment is immense. And just as the state of one from whom a dinar is stolen is lighter than the state of one from whom ten dinars are stolen, so too the state of the owner of a dirham is lighter than the state of the owner of two dirhams—and that is the meaning of His saying, "The owner of a dirham is lighter in account than the owner of two dirhams." And there is nothing of the world that remains with you at death except that it becomes a regret upon you after death. So if you wish, amass much, and if you wish, take little. For if you amass much, you are but amassing regret; and if you take little, you are not lightening [your burden] except by little.

Save from your own self, for indeed snakes and scorpions multiply in the graves of the wealthy who preferred the worldly life over the Hereafter, rejoiced in it, and found contentment therein. These are the stations of faith amidst the serpents and scorpions of the grave and all other forms of its torment. Abu Sa'id al-Khudri saw his son, who had died, in a dream and said to him, "O my son, admonish me." He replied, "Do not oppose God Almighty in what He wills." He said, "O my son, give me more." He said, "O my father, you cannot bear it." He said, "Speak." He said, "Do not place a shirt between you and God." So he did not wear a shirt for thirty years.

If you ask: Which of these three stations is correct? Know that among people are those who affirm only the first and deny what follows; among them are those who deny the first and affirm the second; and among them are those who affirm only the third. The truth that has been unveiled to us through insight is that all of that is within the realm of possibility, and whoever denies any of it does so due to the narrowness of his comprehension and his ignorance of the vastness of God's power, glorified be He, and the wonders of His management. Thus, he denies from the acts of God Almighty what he is not familiar with and accustomed to, and that is ignorance and deficiency. Rather, these three ways of punishment are possible, and believing in them is obligatory. Some servants are punished with one type of these kinds, and some servants have all three kinds combined upon them. We seek refuge in God from His punishment, little or much. This is the truth, so believe in it by following tradition, for it is rare upon the face of the earth for one to know it with certainty. What I advise you is not to excessively ponder the details of that, nor occupy yourself with knowing it. Rather, occupy yourself with planning how to ward off the punishment in any way. For if you neglect work and worship and occupy yourself with investigating that, you are like one seized by a ruler and imprisoned to have his hand cut off and his nose severed, who spends the entire night pondering whether he will cut it with a knife, a sword, or a razor, while neglecting the means of cunning to avert the root of the punishment from himself. This is the height of ignorance, for it is known with certainty that after death, the servant is not free from either immense punishment or everlasting bliss, so one must prepare for it. As for investigating the details of punishment and reward, it is superfluous and a waste of time.

(Explanation of the questioning by Munkar and Nakir, their forms, the pressure of the grave, and the remainder of the discourse on the torment of the grave)
